REASONS FOR DECISION
Introduction
Complaint dated 4 March 2009 initially came before a differently constituted
Tribunal (the initial Tribunal). That Tribunal’s
decision (as set out in
was the subject of an appeal to the Supreme
Court of New South Wales (
NSWSC 1220).
was only partially successful and the matter was remitted on a limited basis to
a differently constituted tribunal. The
Short Minutes of Order formed volume 1,
Tribunal was constituted in accordance with the direction of the Supreme Court
to determine the question of protective orders
that should flow from the finding
of professional misconduct (Complaint Two) that was the undisturbed finding of
the initial Tribunal.
to the separate Complaint of convictions rendering the Respondent unfit in the
public interest within the meaning of section 39(2)(a)
Pharmacy Practice Act
(Complaint Three) there was little comment in the decision of the
Supreme Court. The parties initially both made submissions to the
effect that it
was open for this Tribunal to make findings with respect to this Complaint. The
HCCC later waivered on this position.
Complaint was amended with the consent of both parties. The amendments were of a
minor nature bringing the Complaint into line
with the evidence as it came
before the initial Tribunal. It is the Amended Complaint that is before this
the initial Tribunal commenced under the
Pharmacy Practice Act 2006
Inquiry was conducted (by virtue of the transitional provisions) under the
Health Practitioner Regulation National Law (NSW)
No86a (National Law).
After consideration of the transitional provisions it was the submission of both
parties that this matter should
proceed under the National Law.
a result of the implementation of the Orders made in the Supreme Court a number
of reports and documents came into evidence before
the Tribunal. The final
hearing day of the Inquiry became necessary in order for the Tribunal to
properly deal with the issue of
the weekly reports that the Tribunal continued
to receive from the Respondent’s supervisor (Mr Cohen). It was during the
Hearing held to hear the parties as to the appropriate course to be
taken with respect to these ongoing reports that the Tribunal
set a timetable
for the final service of documents and indicated no further weekly reports were
to be taken into evidence.

The Law – preliminary matters
Standard of proof required
the standard of proof required to establish a complaint in this jurisdiction is
essentially the civil standard, because of
the seriousness of the allegations
and the gravity of their consequences, the Tribunal must be ‘comfortably
that the particulars of the Complaint have been established.
This qualifies the civil standard that is applied by the Tribunal.
v Briginshaw
[1938] HCA 34
(1938) 60 CLR 336
, Dixon J (as he then
was) at 362–363 said:
‘The seriousness of an allegation made, the inherent
unlikelihood of an occurrence of a given description, or the gravity of
consequences flowing from a particular finding are considerations which must
affect the answer to the question whether the issue
has been proved to the
reasonable satisfaction of the tribunal. In such matters ‘reasonable
satisfaction’ should not
be produced by inexact proofs, indefinite
testimony, or indirect inferences ... This does not mean that some standard of
is fixed intermediate between the satisfaction beyond reasonable
doubt required upon a criminal inquest and the reasonable satisfaction
a civil issue may, not must, be based on a preponderance of probability. It
means that the nature of the issue necessarily
affects the process by which
reasonable satisfaction is attained.’
Nature of the jurisdiction
Wentworth v New South Wales Bar Association
[1992] HCA 24
(1992) 176 CLR
at 250-251). The object is not to punish the practitioner but to protect
the public and to maintain proper standards in the profession.
New South Wales Bar Association
[1960] HCA 40
(1960) 104 CLR 186
at 201-202, the High
Court said:
Although it is sometimes referred to as the ‘penalty of
disbarment’ it must be emphasised that a disbarring order is in
punitive in character. When such an order is made, it is made, from the public
point of view, for the protection of those
who require protection, and from the
professional point of view, in order that abuse of privilege may not lead to
loss of privilege.
Tribunal not only has to protect the public but it is in the community interest
that the profession of pharmacy should retain
and be entitled to retain the
respect and high regard in which it is held. Deterrence is also a relevant
trust placed in pharmacists is analogous to that placed in medical practitioners
because of the highly personal information that
the pharmacist comes to know by
virtue of the role a pharmacist plays in the provision of medication to the
patient. In a passage
cited with approval in
Health Care Complaints
Commission v Litchfield
[1997] NSWSC 297
(1997) 41 NSWLR 630
at 638, Priestley JA said in
Richter v Walton
(Court of Appeal, 15 September 1993, unreported):
The degree of trust which patients necessarily give to their
doctors may vary according to the condition which takes the patient to
doctor. Even in regard to the most commonplace medical matters, the trust a
patient places in a doctor is considerable. In
some cases, of which the present
seems to be an example, the patient’s trust cannot help but be almost
absolute. The doctor’s
power in regard to the patient in such cases is
also very great. I do not mean power in the abstract way but as a matter of
the extent of the power will vary according to the temperament of the
patient, but the doctor with some patients and for limited
periods, because of
the relationship in which they are temporarily placed, is in a position to do
whatever the doctor wants with
the body of the patient. This is one of the
reasons why doctors are subject to correspondingly great obligations and are
to maintain high standards; all this being very much in the public
the primary role of the Tribunal is protective, it also has a role in
maintaining public confidence in the profession and
maintaining the reputation
of the profession. Orders of the Tribunal may operate to have a general
deterrent effect for other members
of the profession.
cases of assistance are referred to under the heading of Submissions below.

parties were represented as per the headnotes throughout the Inquiry (Mr

Background information
Background of the Respondent
Respondent arrived in Australia in 1990. The Respondent has suffered from
panhypopituitarism since childhood. After working as
an employee in a community
pharmacy, he set up his own pharmacy in its current location in 1992. Apart from
the six months during
2010 when the Respondent did not hold registration as a
result of the initial Tribunal’s Order, the Respondent has practiced
continuously.
Respondent was first registered in New South Wales in 1987 (volume 2, exhibit
2). The Register held on behalf of the Pharmacy
Board of Australia records that
the Respondent received his BSc (Pharm) from ‘Brighton Tech’. The
Respondent gave evidence
that he was initially registered in Queensland.
Queensland did not require him to undertake a further examination at the time he
registered and his New South Wales registration was granted on the basis of
mutual recognition.
background of the Respondent’s criminal convictions is set out in the
Submissions of the Respondent commencing at paragraph
80. Details are also
recorded in the Chronology provided by the HCCC (volume 6, tab 1). The
Respondent was prosecuted under
section 9(1)(b)
Poisons and
Therapeutic Goods Act 1966
on 7 July 1995. He was again prosecuted on 9
December 1998. This related to 23 charges under the provisions of the
and Therapeutic Goods Act 1966
On 2 February 2007 the Respondent was again
prosecuted under the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods Act 1966
Confirmation of Registration Status dated 6 May 2011 (volume 6, exhibit 5)
records the Respondent was registered until 31 December
2011 and that from 5
November 2011 various conditions applied to his registration.
Background of the Complaint
current complaint arose out of the Respondent’s conduct, subject of his
most recent criminal convictions.
Evidence before the Tribunal
Tribunal had a vast amount of evidence before it. Documentation included all
those exhibits before the initial Tribunal, the decision
of the Supreme Court as
well as material (substantially statements of the Respondent and various reports
regarding his practice)
generated since the time of the Appeal.
was a large amount of documentation before the Tribunal. The Tribunal does not
make any attempt to summarise the evidence in
its entirety. Rather the evidence
is highlighted below and then dealt with by reference to the submissions made by
the parties.

(PSU)/Pharmaceutical Services Branch of NSW Health
(PSB) attended the
Respondent’s pharmacy for the purpose of ascertaining compliance with the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods
Legislation. The report produced by the PSB was
dated 6 May 2011. A letter in evidence indicates that this report was forwarded
the HCCC on the same date.
summary of the findings of this inspection is found in paragraph 118 of the
Respondent’s Submissions (reproduced in Annexure
respect to the display of the wrong Pharmacist in Charge identification the
Tribunal notes that although there was conflicting
evidence regarding the last
date on which Mr Sidney Ho worked in the pharmacy, he had ceased his employment
by 29 March 2011 or 4
April 2011. The Tribunal also notes that the inspection
identified conduct of the Respondent that breached the Therapeutic Goods
legislation.
Tribunal also notes that in his oral evidence the Respondent identified the
white powder found in an unlabelled jar in the dispensary
to be salicylic acid.
PSU - Report dated 15 September 2011
Tribunal became aware of a report authored by Ms Dolan dated 15 September 2011
at the further Directions Hearing held to deal
with the continuing receipt of
weekly reports from Mr Cohen. Ms Dolan’s report was served in accordance
with the timetable
set during the further Directions Hearing.
report details reports and subsequent investigations made by Ms Dolan following
a report from an anaesthetist at a large Sydney
hospital (hospital). The
anaesthetist had telephoned Ms Dolan on 13 September 2011 stating that he had
observed a patient in a ward
‘off his face’
had been dispensed by the Respondent on the
prescription of a GP. The
anaesthetist further stated that he had contacted the Respondent who had
informed him that he had delivered
the medication to the patient in the waiting
room of the emergency department of the hospital as the patient was unable to
the pharmacy.
following day Ms Dolan attended the Respondent’s pharmacy and inspected
his drug register. Ms Dolan expressed her concern
to the Respondent that a
Schedule 8 medication had been delivered to an inpatient in a public hospital.
The Respondent stated that
the patient was an inpatient in the burns unit of the
hospital and that he did not see a problem with delivering the medication along
with other items such as shampoo and other toiletries.
Respondent informed Ms Dolan that the GP’s secretary had delivered the
script from the GP’s surgery to the pharmacy
on the day it was dispensed.
Dolan’s report then records Mr Cohen arrived at the pharmacy at this stage
of the conversation and Ms Dolan explained the
scenario to him. Ms Dolan records
the following interaction occurred:
Mr Cohen asked where in the legislation it says that you can’t deliver
medicines to an inpatient in a hospital. The conversation
heated up. Mr Cohen
said that Mr Ng had done nothing wrong, he had a legal prescription and he
delivered it. I explained although
it was not illegal, that professional
judgement and common sense sometimes need to be used, and I felt that Mr Ng had
used poor judgment
in this situation. I explained that when you are an inpatient
in a hospital, the hospital provides you with your medications and
it could be
detrimental if patients took medications without the hospital aware of this.
15 September 2011 Ms Dolan attended upon the prescribing GP. He stated that he
had had a conversation with the Respondent regarding
the prescription in
question but had not been advised that the medication was delivered to a public
hospital. He had asked his secretary
to walk the script up to the
Respondent’s pharmacy as the patient was in a wheelchair as he was a
paraplegic following injuries
sustained in a motorbike accident.
Dolan concludes her report as follows:
Mr Ng’s actions in providing Schedule 8 drugs of addiction to an
inpatient of a public hospital without the knowledge of his
treating medical
practitioner at RNSH has risked the health of the patient and undermined and
impeded their treatment plans for this
Oral Evidence of Ms Dolan
Dolan is a registered pharmacist currently working as the Acting Principal
Pharmaceutical Officer – Inspections and Investigations,
Pharmaceutical
Services Unit, Department of Health. Ms Dolan gave evidence regarding an
inspection of the Respondent’s pharmacy
she undertook with Ms Reyes, a
Pharmaceutical Officer on 15 April 2011.
consideration of this witness’ evidence is required in order to gain a
full appreciation of her evidence in chief as
it was so frequently interrupted
by objections.
Dolan described the manner in which the report was prepared.
Dolan gave evidence about the meaning of an AustR and AustL numbers and their
significance with respect to the practice of pharmacy.
Dolan described the location of the keys for the Drug Safe being in an unlocked
draw along with other keys.
Dolan then provided further details of the matters detailed in the report
including her view that the error in the recording of
OxyContin® was not in
the nature of a ‘human error’.
answer to questions regarding the time taken to identify the out of date stock,
Ms Dolan stated that it had taken a couple of hours
for the two of them to
locate those items listed in the report.
Dolan gave further evidence on 31 May 2012. Under cross-examination Ms Dolan was
asked a number of questions regarding the authority
prescription for
OxyContin® (40mg). The Tribunal notes at this point that earlier
prescriptions involving this particular patient
were also subject of some of the
Particulars of the Complaint in this matter.
Dolan gave evidence that she agreed that ‘on the face of it’ the
script appeared to be valid. She further agreed that
if the assumption was made
that the patient was taking the medication as prescribed that the patient must
have had other prescriptions
filled at a pharmacy other than the
Respondent’s. This view was reinforced by the fact that the particular
medication was addictive
in nature making it less likely that the patient would
be going for periods without the medication. Ms Dolan also expressed the view
that considering the dispensing history available to the Respondent she would
assume that this was not a ‘commencement’
dose of the medication for
this particular patient. Ms Dolan was unable to agree with the proposition put
during cross-examination
that the patient must have been obtaining prescriptions
from a doctor other than the one named on the scripts held by the Respondent.

following exchange concluded Ms Dolan’s cross-examination (T551-552):

answer to questions from the Tribunal Ms Dolan agreed that a pharmacist needs to

Reports of Mr Mahony
Thomas Mahony is a registered pharmacist. He initially provided a peer review
report (February 2010 – volume 4, exhibit
4) and gave evidence before the
initial Tribunal. As a result of the undertaking made by the Respondent to the
Supreme Court, Mr
Mahony became the Respondent’s business mentor in
accordance with the undertaking made to the court on 27 October 2010.
February 2010 report included the following ‘Executive Summary’:
I have been requested by Nr Ng’s solicitor to provide a peer review of
his pharmacy. The report and recommendations below are
made in accordance with
the Expert Witness Code of Conduct.
I attended Mr Ng’s pharmacy at Ryde on Thursday 18 February and
interviewed Mr Ng and his staff. I also observed the operations
pharmacy. I have also read the various documents included in this
Despite every effort to avoid prejudicing the situation, when I read the
three volumes of documents, I subconsciously formed an opinion
that Mr Ng was
wilful in his attempts to achieve some personal gain in assisting various client
Continuing to
provide Controlled Drugs in a quantity and for a purpose that were not within
the therapeutic recommendations
record mandated transactions for the possible attempt to disguise some form of
illegal activity
large quantities of an item in a significant single transaction (30x Cialis @
approximately $70ea on 30 November 2004) which
were outside the norm for a
suburban pharmacy and
Attempting to
gain by fraudulently constructing transactions so as to overstate the
pharmacy’s performance vis generic sales.
As a result of my visit to the pharmacy I have formed the opinion that in the
case of the first three points, Mr Ng was misguided
in his attempts to assist
his clients. In some cases he may have deliberately flouted the regulations to
assist a client. Mr Ng’s
actions are not condoned in any way and do show
a lack of judgment and care.
I am unable to conclude the same with item #4, where this was a deliberate
attempt for personal gain. I can only assume this was
due to his paranoia
regarding the advantages gained by bigger pharmacy retailers in particular the
“discount pharmacies”.
I am unsure if such practices are widespread
and I condemn them as unethical.
My observations during my visit to the pharmacy and discussions with Mr Ng
and his staff are that Mr Ng is most anxious that he has
re-offended and is most
remorseful. Mr Ng also accepts that since the 14 February 2001 Pharmacy Board
decision and implementation
of its orders, he has not changed his business and
professional practices sufficiently to prevent a recurrence of the problems that
bring him before this Board of Inquiry.
My observations of the pharmacy are that it is not functioning professionally
and cannot continue in its present mode. I could not
therefore provide a
positive peer report. I do however make a number of recommendations to provide
a framework for Mr Ng to continue
to practice as a pharmacist without being a
risk to the public.
Mr Ng, I believe, failed to comply with the earlier orders of the Board due
to a lack of commitment to change, some difficult business
circumstances (due to
the reconstruction of the Top Ryde Centre), a lack of business acumen and
experience in the Australian ethic/professional
culture and/or insufficient
support to achieve the orders set out by the
Mr Ng has maintained his DD Register in an accurate manner since his
interview with Mr Battye and Mr Gavrilovic in February
However, I now further believe that Mr Ng has realised that he must change
and that he is committed to a plan that will enable him
to continue to practice
as a pharmacist, in a profession he loves and in the hope to be able to share
his profession with his daughter,
who starts Pharmacy at Sydney University in
Mr Mahony’s recommendations to Mr Ng
Ng engages a Business Mentor to oversee the implementation of a business plan.
This plan would address all of the issues that
have been contributory to Mr
Ng’s failures and will include certain quantifiable benchmarks and a
reasonable time table.
7 days of this Inquiry, he will provide a contract of employment of the said
mentor to the Board.
14 days of this Inquiry, he will have employed additional support staff for the
pharmacy and to change his work practices so
as to be able to commit the
resources necessary to meet this schedule.
Ng provides within 3 months a plan that will establish, to the satisfaction of
an independent auditor (list of qualified persons
to be supplied for nomination
by the Board), the following;
Business structure to focus on the protection of the public, the professional
standards of the pharmacy and the work load of Mr
staffing policy to engage additional support staff to provide a standard of
service consistent with a professional pharmacy
business plan that is sufficiently viable to sustain the commitment and to fund
delivery of this audit.
Ng continues to work with the mentor to provide independent audit reports to the
Pharmacy Board of the implementation of this plan
at 6 months, 9 months, 12
months, 18 months and 24 months.
Ng will undertake additional training (professional, managerial and
workplace)(self-paced and/or course based) as deemed necessary
by the mentor to
achieve his commitment to the Board.

final report from Mr Mahony dated 7 December 2011 was received by the Tribunal
for the final hearing day on 31 May 2012. Mr Mahony
also provided a statement
dated 12 April 2012 (volume R4- exhibit 36). Mr Mahony also gave further
evidence on this occasion.
his statement dated 12 April 2012 Mr Mahony substantially dealt with the issues
arising from the Report of Ms Dolan dated 15 September
2011 regarding the
Respondent’s dispensing of medications, including OxyContin, to a patient
in the grounds of a hospital on
8 September 2011.
Mahony stated at paragraph 10 that he had reviewed the prescription for the
OxyContin® and was of the view that it was
‘capable of being
legally dispensed’
by the Respondent.
Mahony also considered that the prescriptions for Lioresal, Microlax® and
Valpam® (being the other medications dispensed
to the patient on 8
September 2011) were
‘capable of being legally dispensed’
paragraph 12 Mr Mahony stated that despite this he was of the view that the
Respondent should have exercised the discretion given
to him under clauses 85(1)
and 35(1) of the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods Regulations 2008 (NSW)
declined to dispense the prescriptions and supply the medications outside the
entrance to the Accident/Emergency Department of
a hospital in which the patient
was domiciled. Mr Mahony expressed the view that the Respondent exercised poor
professional judgment
in so doing.
Mahony then sets out reasons why he expresses this view. At this point the
Tribunal notes in particular the matters referred to
in paragraphs 14, 15 and 16
of Mr Mahony’s report and accepts his clear analysis of the relevant
factors. These paragraphs
read as follows:
Mr Ng ought to have been aware that in a public
patient’s medication (including any regular prescription, pharmacist-only
or over-the-counter medication) is prescribed by
the medical practitioner(s)
responsible for the care of the patient while he/she is an inpatient at the
patient’s medication (including any regular prescription, pharmacist-only
or over-the-counter medication) is dispensed through
the hospital pharmacy and
the supply of that medication to the patient is strictly controlled by the
nurses and medical practitioners
responsible for the care of the patient while
he/she is an inpatient at the hospital;
patient is typically not permitted to self-medicate and would certainly not be
permitted to self-medicate Schedule 8 drugs such
as oxycodone hydrochloride
(OxyContin®); and
patient’s medication that he/she has brought with him/her to the hospital
will only be used by the hospital in treating the
patient if the hospital does
not currently have that medication or its generic equivalent on hand, in which
case the patient’s
medication will be withdrawn from use as soon as the
hospital has the relevant medication in stock.
Mr Ng ought to have been aware that in a public hospital the nurse in charge of
the relevant ward is responsible for the storage
of all medication on the ward,
and all Schedule 4 and Schedule 8 drugs on the ward are required by regulations
30(1) and 75(1) of
the Regulations to be stored in a separate locked room, safe,
cupboard or other receptacle that is securely attached to a part of
Further, even in circumstances where a patient is permitted to self-medicate and
store their medication in their lockable
bedside cabinet, that patient would not
be permitted to store Schedule 8 drugs in their lockable bedside cabinet due to
the security
and recording requirements of those drugs.
the dispensing pattern of controlled release tablets of OxyContin® to
[the patient]
at Ryde Pharmacy strongly suggests that this medication was
also being dispensed to
[the patient]
at other pharmacies and possibly on
prescriptions issued by a number of different medical practitioners.
Mahony also undertook an analysis of the pattern of dispensing of OxyContin®
to the patient from the Respondent’s pharmacy
and his conclusions
therefrom in paragraphs 17 to 24. Once again the Tribunal accepts Mr
Mahony’s clear analysis. These paragraphs
read as follows:

identification and recording of those drugs (T348-349). When pressed by senior
counsel for the Respondent

at July 2011 Mr Piggott expressed the view that he considered the Respondent to

1956. Mr Dash also confirmed that he is not a registered pharmacist, having let

to fill in the gaps

asked if he had produced an examination to test the Respondent’s

to know and that were related to his brief. Mr
Dash also stated that these requirements related to issues regarding the
of medicines in Schedules 2, 3, 4 and 8 of the Poisons List, and that
from the written answers to the questions he thought that the
Respondent’s

practising pharmacist.
Dash stated that although the Respondent had been practising pharmacy for some
21 years he was not concerned that there were gaps
Respondent’s knowledge which Mr Dash had had to fill in. Mr Dash stated
‘Everyone will have an occasional gap in their knowledge, as
circumstances change, or legislation is updated.’
Dash stated that the fact the Respondent did not have the required legislation
(National Law New South Wales) was of concern to
him, but that the Respondent
did have a current copy of the
Guide to the New South Wales Medicines and
Poisons Schedules,
which summarised the legislative issues which are of
relevance to a practising community pharmacist. He stated that the Respondent
did not explain to him why he did not have a copy of the required legislation,
and that he accordingly provided the Respondent with
various pieces of
legislation which a pharmacist is required to have on hand.
Dash stated that from the Respondent's examination results he had a good working
knowledge of his professional obligations but
he could not offer a view as to
how much of that working knowledge could be attributed to the tutoring sessions
he had provided to
the Respondent, because he had not ascertained the
Respondent’s level of knowledge in detail prior to the examination.

obligations in various
circumstances.

previous history and that he was only now aware of
the Respondent’s
convictions relating to non-compliance with legislative requirements. He
confirmed that he was aware that
the Respondent had been the subject of
disciplinary proceedings for failure to comply with legislative requirements.
He also confirmed
that it would be a matter of concern to him that the
Respondent had been the subject of previous adverse findings regarding his
with legislative requirements.
Dash stated that there were some notations on the Respondent’s examination
paper indicating important information that had
been omitted by the Respondent.
He stated, however, that under examination conditions people can fail to record
everything they
know as they are forgetful, and the Respondent’s
examination result may not necessarily be a reflection of his knowledge of
legislative requirements.
Dash stated that the examination was equivalent to that expected of a fourth
year pharmacy student. He stated that he would expect
the Respondent to have a
more practical knowledge base of legislative requirements (given the length of
time he has practised pharmacy)
than a fourth year pharmacy student, and that a
fourth year pharmacy student would probably be more specific regarding their
legislative
obligations.
Dash confirmed that the Respondent’s examination paper was not a copy of
one used for fourth year pharmacy students (as none
were available to Mr Dash at
the time) but one Mr Dash prepared that covered the issues that were of
importance.

they agree to take a portion of it then I would enter in the
computer and enter
in the DD registry whatever was given and then order it at the same
Q. I see. If for example a customer came in with a script requiring 60
tablets of drug X, and you only had 40 tablets in the safe,
and assume that the
patient was prepared to simply take 40 at that point in time, what would you
enter into the drug register?
A. The drug register is 40.
Q. I see. You wouldn't enter the 60 which was for the whole
prescription?
A. I don't have the 60 in stock.
Q. Is that the reason why you would only enter 40 in the--
A. Yes, I enter 40 and then later on when the stock comes in, I enter
another 20 after that.

Dr Peter Klug
Klug is a psychiatrist that examined the Respondent at the request of his legal
advisors on 31 March 2011. The letter of instruction
to Dr Klug does not mention
the issue of unfitness to practice on the basis of convictions. Dr Klug prepared
a report dated 27 April
2011 (volume 2, exhibit 6). The report records that the
Respondent is working as a pharmacist under the supervision of Mr Ho three
per week and when Mr Ho is not in the pharmacy the Respondent was not working as
the pharmacist and another pharmacist performed
Klug records that the Respondent informed him that the complaint relates to his
failure to keep up to date with DD Register paper
work due to being overworked.
The Respondent informed Dr Klug that he had not been having his testosterone
injections regularly around
the time the inspection found the DD Register was
not up to date.
Respondent reported to Dr Klug that amongst other changes he was now making
every effort to keep his business under control and
taking regular medication
including the testosterone injections. Dr Klug recorded that due to his
panhypopituitarism the Respondent
needed to take various hormones in order to
function normally. These hormones were thyroxine (orally), hydrocortisone
(orally) and
testosterone (intramuscularly every two to three weeks).
Respondent informed Dr Klug that as soon as staffing levels were appropriate he
anticipated being able to work as a pharmacist
on his own again.
Klug recorded that the Respondent’s GP was Dr Soh who provided the
hormonal supplements and monitored the Respondent’s
hormonal parameters.
At this point the Tribunal notes it did not receive any evidence from Dr Soh. Dr
Klug also recorded that the
Respondent’s endocrinologist was Professor
David Hendelsman but that he had not needed to see him for many years.
Klug recorded under the heading of personal history that the Respondent had no
record of criminal offences. While indicating that
he did not intend to pre-empt
the Tribunal’s decision Dr Klug expressed the view that the excessive
lethargy and fatigue suffered
by the Respondent as a result of failure to take
regular testosterone injections was a contributing factor, if not a mitigating
with respect to the complaint.
report then addresses specific questions posed by the Respondent’s legal
advisers as follows:
In response to your specific questions:

to the safety of the

provided an open and spontaneous
account of events and expressed remorse and
contrition without my providing any leading

he has continued to be distressed

depression of his mood, at no time has he suffered from any frank psychiatric

program and business plan
Mr Ng’s attitude toward the implementation and progress of the

is happy to comply
with it until the program and plan has been completed. He understands that both
the program and plan have been,
and will be beneficial to him and his
opinion as to Mr Ng’s capacity to continue to comply with the supervision
program and business plan from a psychological
perspective
Mr Ng clearly plans to continue to comply with the supervision program and
business plan, and from a psychiatric perspective, has
the capacity to do
Dr Klug – oral evidence
Klug gave evidence by telephone on 11 May 2011 (T141-162).
his oral evidence Dr Klug confirmed that he is a clinical and forensic
psychiatrist and that he prepared a report relating
to the Respondent dated
27 April 2011.
Klug stated that he interviewed the Respondent on 30 March 2011, on
instructions from Gadens Lawyers. Dr Klug did not feel that
there was a need
for any follow-up consultation for the purposes of preparing the report.
Klug stated that the Respondent made it clear to him that he was working as a
supervised pharmacist.
Klug confirmed that his report did not reference the Tribunal's Decision from
April 2010 which made findings of professional misconduct
in relation to the
Respondent, and he also accepted that this had not been provided and or
received. He conceded that it may have
this document could have been of assistance to him in forming his views of the
Respondent.
Klug stated that, from his consultation with the Respondent, the Respondent now
understood the importance of ensuring that entries
in the DD register were
properly made and that the Respondent now appreciated the importance of
complying with auditing requirements
in terms of the DD drug register.
was Dr Klug’s impression from his consultation with the Respondent that
the Respondent’s difficulties with his DD register
occurred over a
relatively short period of time (weeks). Although not stated by the Respondent,
it was Dr Klug’s impression
that these difficulties occurred when the
Respondent was particularly unwell because of his pituitary disease.
Klug’s ‘impression’ as to the reason why the Respondent failed
on occasions to have his testosterone injections
as frequently as he should have
was that the Respondent was working hard and he didn't find the time to go to
Klug agreed that it was his opinion that one of the possible mitigating (and
contributing) factors in relation to the previous
complaints was the fact that
the Respondent was not having his testosterone injections at the required
frequency, which could have
contributed to the Respondent being tired and
lethargic, together with him working long hours. Dr Klug stated that there was
no validation
process (e.g. GP file notes) used, to his knowledge, to ascertain
whether the Respondent was currently
having his testosterone
injections with sufficient frequency.
Klug confirmed that he had been given an incomplete forensic history by the
Respondent (Tribunal appearance in 1995 when it was
in fact a Local Court
appearance, omission of Local Court appearance in 1998 and omission of Pharmacy
Board of Inquiry in 2000 into
allegations of professional misconduct).
Klug stated that it could possibly be of concern to him if the Respondent, on
previous occasions, had been found not to comply
with legislative requirements
which are imposed on pharmacists. In particular, whether ‘
the reasons
for any such non-compliance are related in any way to his history of pituitary
disease and the consequences of that, and
irregularly getting treatment, because
pituitary problems like that have a very high association with psychiatric
’. He stated that the Respondent made no excuses (including
his pituitary disease) to him regarding his breaches of legislative
obligations.
Klug stated that it was the Respondent who advised him that he was to appear
again before the Pharmacy Tribunal on 5 November 2011.
notes are unclear as to whether the date is 5 November 2011 or May 2011, as he
had noted ‘
’. Dr Klug thought he had made a mistake in
dictation from his notes and had concluded it was May 2011.
Klug stated that if he had been informed by the Respondent that he had a
criminal history relating to convictions with respect
to breaches of the
regulations dealing with the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods Act
be of some significance. Dr Klug stated that breaches of regulations on multiple
occasions spread out over a period of time
would be relevant to him, as would be
being aware of any conditions that had been placed on the Respondent’s
practice by a
Board of Inquiry and if they were breached. He agreed that if any
of these matters had been known to him, they would possibly have
influenced the
opinions expressed in his report.
Klug confirmed that he had overlooked the criminal convictions noted in the
Supreme Court Decision (Adams J, 22 October 2010) and
listed in his
Klug stated that there had been issues within the Respondent’s marriage
around 2006 which related to his convictions. He
stated that he thought the
Respondent’s significant medical health issue was related to his
non-compliance.
Dr Klug stated that registered health practitioners have an obligation to take
care of their health to ensure that they practice
safely. He also stated that
the Respondent is now taking better care of his health by having regular health
checks (including regular
administration of testosterone injections and
influenza vaccinations).
Klug explained that pan-hypopituitarism has a high association with psychiatric
illness particularly if not well managed; most
commonly, depressive illnesses.
He stated this can be marked and people can be irritable, have reduced drive and
initiative, and
inertia, and that people can be abnormally indifferent to
things. He stated that physical problems include impotence, weight loss
changes, all of which can contribute to anxiety. Dr Klug stated that if a
person has inconsistent effective replacement
therapy then there is a high
likelihood of psychiatric problems (most likely depressive forms).
was Dr Klug’s opinion that it would be ‘
a good idea
for the Respondent to be cared for by someone in addition to a general
practitioner, such as an endocrinologist. He stated
that the Respondent had
said that he ‘
was technically under the care of an endocrinologist
(Professor David Hendelsman/RPA – name taken from Dr Klug’s report,
incorrectly transcribed as Higgins) but hadn't seen one for some time given that
his GP was treating him
Klug agreed that if reports were furnished from supervisors about the Respondent
indicating inertia, lack of drive, etc., this
would be of concern and may
indicate partial treatment of the Respondent’s pan-hypopituitarism.
Klug stated that the Respondent informed him that his wife had been a pharmacy
technician in Hong Kong.

Respondent gave evidence as to his use of the terms dispensing and supply.
Dispensing involved putting the prescription through
the computer system and
supply involved providing the drugs to the patient. The Respondent accepted that
Clause 112 of the Poisons
and Therapeutic Goods Regulations required a record to
be made in the DD Book on the day on which the Schedule 8 drug was supplied
the patient. Later in his evidence the Respondent gave evidence that appeared to
contradict his evidence regarding his practice
regarding when the record was
made in the DD Book (i.e. at dispensing or supply – see T 89). The
Respondent also stated that
as at April 2011 he did not appreciate his
obligations under Clause 112 of the Regulations (T92).

Tribunal accepts the submissions made in paragraphs 4, 5 and 6 of the HCCC

Tribunal accepts the submission made in paragraph 7 of the HCCC written
submissions. The holding of registration is a privilege
not a right. The
comments made by the Pharmacy Board in the matter of
NSWPB 8) emphasise that there are many procedures, protocols and validations in
place in the practice of pharmacy to act
as reminders to practitioners of the
responsibility of pharmacy ownership. At this point the Tribunal also notes that
many authorities
have acknowledged that the orders resulting from misconduct in
a protective jurisdiction are sometimes more onerous than those that
result from a jurisdiction aimed at punishment for a breach of the relevant
legislation as the role of each jurisdiction is
quite different.
Tribunal accepts the submission made in paragraphs 8 of the HCCC written
submissions, noting that compliance with legal obligations
is an expectation
placed upon all registered health practitioners.
Tribunal notes and accepts the contents of paragraphs 9, 10, 11 and 12 of the
HCCC submissions.
indicated during the Inquiry the Tribunal accepts the submission of both parties
that the applicable law is the National Law (paragraph
13 HCCC of the
Submissions) and that the powers of the Tribunal to make protective orders are
set out in sections 149A – 149C
of the National Law paragraph 14 HCCC of
the Submissions).

the submission made in paragraphs 1 and 2.

Tribunal’s ‘conclusions’ and the
evidence upon which they were
based in paragraphs 3 to 7 inclusive. The Tribunal accepts the submissions made
in these paragraphs
as an accurate reflection of the initial Tribunal’s

43 drugs of addition to deal with and in this
Particular he failed to stocktake
19 of them.
initial Tribunal found:
Mr Ng showed a lack of care and judgment in the practice of

pharmacist with several years’ experience in a community pharmacy. His
failure in this regard would attract the serious
disapproval of pharmacists of
good repute and that Mr Ng's failure invited Mr Vincent's strong
criticism",
submissions were made regarding the role of the Respondent’s illness with
respect to the failures evident in this finding.
The Tribunal notes the
obligation on all health practitioners to ensure they are mentally and
physically able to practice safely
and appropriately and to work within their
limitations. Further comments on this matter are set out below.
submissions were also made regarding the time that had passed since the
Respondent’s conduct as detailed in this Particular.
The Tribunal is of
the view that the mere effluxion of time does not remedy defects of this type.
Further, in March 2011 the Respondent
was still failing to meet regulatory
requirements. The Tribunal also notes the evidence of the Respondent regarding
role in the deficits identified in the PSU Audit undertaken on 15
April 2011.
respect to Particular 2, the Respondent made submissions as to the initial

based in paragraphs 8 to 14 inclusive. The Tribunal accepts the submissions

Tribunal notes that the initial Tribunal considered the conduct set out in the
Particular to be of a more serious nature. The
Tribunal notes that some of the
failures to undertake a stock take would have occurred on two occasions –
the legislative requirements
being for a stock take to occur in both March and
September of each year.
Tribunal also notes that the failure to record in the DD register as detailed in
this Particular commenced on 2 February 2004.
The Respondent’s
‘explanation’ of these failures (as detailed in his letter to the
HCCC dated 21 May 2007 –
volume 4 – exhibit 48 – page 163) was
that he became ill in the winter of 2004 which created the backlog of entries
he would deal with when he felt better
‘the following
. The Tribunal is of the view that this may have been an
explanation as to why the DD register was not up to date for a short period
during the winter of 2004 but does not explain conduct that occurred over the
period 2 February 2004 to 6 December 2004. It certainly
does not excuse a
departure from legislative requirements.
respect to Particular 3, the Respondent made submissions as to the initial
Tribunal’s ‘conclusions’ and the
evidence upon which they were
based in paragraphs 15 to 20 inclusive. The Tribunal accepts the submissions
made in these paragraphs
as an accurate reflection of the initial
Tribunal’s comments and findings.
again the Tribunal notes the Respondent’s explanation of falling ill
during the winter. The Respondent also indicates a
lack of staff contributed to
his lack of time to make entries in the DD register. Staffing is a matter
properly considered to be
within the control of the owner of a pharmacy. While
matters such as illness and staffing levels may be explanations as to why the
register was not maintained it cannot be accepted as an excuse given the
statutory nature of the requirement to keep the record
contemporaneously. The Tribunal is of the view that this conduct is more than
mere carelessness on the part of the Respondent,
rather it is indicative of
neglect of his professional responsibilities.

Tribunal notes the contents of paragraph 112 of the Respondent’s
submissions. Since November 2010 the steps taken by the
Respondent as his
‘to having a properly functioning pharmacy’

submissions. The Tribunal notes Mr Piggott’s evidence
that he understood
his role was merely to audit the Business Plan and not to undertake an audit of
the pharmacy. The Tribunal notes
the view of Mr Piggott that the Respondent
‘is having his eyes opened about Community Pharmacy in an Australian
business-sense’
but views this in the context of the number of years
that the Respondent has practiced in his own business as a Community Pharmacist
in Australia. The Tribunal accepts Mr Piggott’s assessment of the Business
‘sound, normal and achievable’
. The Tribunal also
accepts that the Respondent has been very accepting of Mr Mahony’s
teaching as observed by Mr Piggott. The
Tribunal also notes that the Respondent
was accepting of the teachings of the supervisors of his practice in 2001 and of
tuition. What is absent is evidence that would satisfy the
Tribunal that the ‘teaching’ the Respondent has received has
remedied the defects in his practice identified in the findings in this matter.
Further, teaching is one thing; learning and applying
the knowledge and
translating that to skill and care are another.
Tribunal notes the contents of paragraph 114 of the Respondent’s

sit well with the findings of the 15 April Audit undertaken by the PSU.

in the absence of any evidence
regarding the actual content of the report, knowledge of the nature of the

from Mr Cox. The Tribunal also notes at this point that
when Mr Dash attended the pharmacy in June 2011 he did not find a copy of
relevant legislation.
Tribunal notes and accepts the contents of paragraph 116 of the
Respondent’s submissions.
Tribunal notes the contents of paragraphs 117 and 118 of the Respondent’s
submissions. The defects identified by the 15
April Audit undertaken by the PSU
should be placed in the context of the Respondent being part way through an
Inquiry by the Tribunal,
his practice being subject to an undertaking made to
the Supreme Court that allowed him to return to practice and at a time that
Respondent was just being allowed to return to unsupervised practice.
Tribunal notes the contents of paragraph 119 of the Respondent’s
submissions. With respect to Mr Mahony’s comment
recorded at paragraph
119(a) the Tribunal notes that his report of April 2011 Mr Mahony talks of the
‘considerable progress’
that the Respondent had made in the
preceding six months. The Tribunal notes that Mr Mahony attributed the progress
to the Respondent
rather than his supervisor. The Tribunal notes that sub
paragraphs 118 (e), (f) and (h) all relate to the DD register. The Tribunal
notes that sub paragraphs 118 (e), (f), (g) and (h) are all of a similar nature
to matters subject of past complaints regarding
the Respondent’s practice.
The issue recorded in sub paragraphs 118 (b) is so fundamental to the practice
of pharmacy that
it is included in the legislation as a ground for a finding of
unsatisfactory professional conduct (section 139D(d) National Law)
a requirement
of the legislation. The Tribunal is of the view that the conduct subject of
paragraph 118(c) of the Respondent’s
submissions is conduct that amounts
to an unacceptable practice and is known to any registered health practitioner
who holds a Schedule
8 authority (e.g. registered nurses).
respect to paragraph 119(b) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that having an indication that
many items had been discussed and
that they are
‘scheduled to be completed within the agreed
is of little assistance to this Tribunal given the matter
must be determined as at the present time. In any case there is scant evidence
that the Respondent has the ability to permanently remedy issues that are drawn
to his attention.
respect to paragraph 119(c) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that it is inappropriate for the
exclusion of
‘inappropriate stock items’
(being items that statutory
provisions exclude from sale in pharmacies in Australia) from the pharmacy to be
achieved after pending
renovation and the subsequent organisation of stock in a
‘logical sequence and the employment of experienced staff’.
The issue of such stock being on the shelves should not have arisen as checks
should have been made prior to the presentation of
the stock for sale. Once the
matter was brought to the Respondent’s attention the priority must be for
registered pharmacists
to comply with statutory provisions. The Respondent
should have been capable of this task, even in an un-renovated pharmacy.
respect to paragraph 119(d) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
accepts that Mr Ho and Mr Lee should have been
the ‘Pharmacist in
Charge’ of the pharmacy at the relevant times. However, in the
circumstances of this matter a responsible
approach would have been for the
Respondent as the proprietor and as a supervised pharmacist working in the
pharmacy to have ensured
these matters were attended to in accordance with the
statutory requirements.
respect to paragraph 119(e) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that little can turn on the issue
of the safe given the evidence
regarding the bulk etc of the original safe.
respect to paragraph 119(f) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that it is relatively minor issue
standing alone. However, the
recording of the Respondent as the dispensing pharmacist when his evidence was
that he was not in fact
the dispensing pharmacist (as a result of an automatic
but reprogrammable computer function), in the context of this matter, takes
the dimension of being another area where the Respondent failed to take care to
put appropriate procedures in place to ensure
errors such as these did not place
him into an inappropriate position.
respect to paragraph 119(g) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
notes that on the evidence before it the Respondent
appears to have been the
only registered pharmacist working in the pharmacy on at least 2, 3 and 5 April
2011. The Tribunal notes
that the Respondent also remained the proprietor of the
pharmacy throughout the relevant time. Both Mr Piggott and Mr Mahony gave
evidence about their roles in running a number of pharmacies simultaneously.
When the evidence of Mr Mahony and Mr Piggott is considered
globally it is clear
that they both oversee the proper operation of their respective groups of
pharmacies (even though they each
had the benefit of family members and long
standing staff members working in the pharmacies). The Tribunal is of the view
conduct of Mr Mahony and Mr Piggott in this regard is an appropriate
standard to be employed whether a registered pharmacist is the
proprietor of one
pharmacy or several pharmacies.
respect to paragraph 119(h) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that it is certainly not best practice
‘inappropriate and excess stock’
on the shelves when that
stock includes out of date products and products not bearing an AustR or an
AustL number. Further, the Tribunal
is of the view that it is not appropriate
practice for such products to remain on display for any period of time. Such
stock should
have been identified and removed as a priority.
Tribunal notes the contents of paragraph 120 of the Respondent’s
submissions. With respect to paragraph 120(a) of the Respondent’s
submissions the Tribunal has no doubt that the Respondent will be more
to not stock products not bearing an AustR or an
AustL number in view of this matter having been drawn to his attention. There is
an expectation that registered pharmacists will be aware of their statutory
obligations. AustR and AustL numbers are fundamental
parts of the regulation of
medicines and other products within Australia.
respect to paragraph 120(b) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
notes that there was no approved supervising pharmacist
working in the pharmacy
on 15 April 2011. Further, the Tribunal is of the view that while it is the
primary responsibility of the
actual Pharmacist in Charge to have their name
plate displayed correctly that it is also an obligation that falls to any
pharmacist that is working at the time.
respect to paragraph 120(c) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that if in fact the Respondent
did not know it was inappropriate
to keep the drug safe key anywhere other than on his person then his knowledge
of matters fundamental
to the practice of pharmacy is lacking to an alarming
respect to paragraph 120(d) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
refers to comments elsewhere re the safe and notes
that this matter has been
respect to paragraph 120(e)(i) of the Respondent’s submissions the
Tribunal notes that while the Respondent was on holidays
as at the date of
dispensing, the label for the medication records the Respondent as the dispenser
of the medication (see attachment
3 to the PSU report dated 6 may 2011- volume
6, exhibit 4).
respect to paragraph 120(e)(ii) of the Respondent’s submissions the
Tribunal notes that Mr Lee was not approved by the
Council as a supervisor for
the Respondent and therefore the Respondent should not have been working as a
pharmacist.
respect to paragraph 120(e)(iii) of the Respondent’s submissions the
Tribunal is of the view that if in fact the Respondent
did not know the correct
manner to ascertain the ‘supply date’ and the ‘dispensing
date’ this is another
indicator that his knowledge of matters fundamental
to the practice of pharmacy is lacking to an alarming level.
respect to paragraph 120(f)(i) and (ii) of the Respondent’s submissions
the Tribunal has elsewhere commented upon the inadequacy
of the use of a rubber
band to address the issue of returned stock, the lack of differentiation between
‘supply’ and
‘dispensing’ date.
respect to paragraph 120(g) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
notes the issue for consideration is who dispensed
the prescription rather than
who ‘issued’ the
‘non-conforming prescription’
respect to paragraph 120(h) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
accepts that ‘human’ errors occur
from time to time in professional
practice. One of the roles of the training required for registration is to
reduce the occurrence
of such errors by identifying the likely areas for such
errors to occur. Fundamental to the counting of medication is the ascertaining
of the number of items in each package. This, of course, is fundamental to any
stock take exercise whether undertaken in accordance
with a statutory provision
or not. Where it is undertaken to fulfil a statutory obligation there is an
expectation that greater care
would be taken.
respect to paragraph 120(i) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
notes that the warnings provided to these patients
was verbal only. The
Respondent’s understanding of the significance of avoiding foods
containing cytochrome P450 enzyme inhibitors
(e.g. grapefruit and grapefruit
juice) when taking certain statins (a type of medication) is another matter that
adds to the Tribunal’s
concerns regarding the Respondent’s knowledge
of matters relevant to the practice of pharmacy (as detailed by the Tribunal
above) and the care that he takes in his practice. The inhibitors found in these
food stuffs can remain in the body for several days
and the interaction with the
statin medication can cause serious harm (such as muscle damage). This is not
merely a matter where
the medication is rendered ineffective or less effective
or may cause a transient side effect such a nausea.
respect to paragraph 120(j) of the Respondent’s submissions the Tribunal
is of the view that a jar of unidentified white
powder should just not have been
in the pharmacy at all, regardless of whether it was for sale or not. The
presence of such a substance
in an area used for the dispensing of medication
for systemic and topical use by humans is inappropriate and contrary to
professional
practice. There clearly was no means by which the identification of
the substance, a batch number or expiry date could be identified.
The presence
of such a substance in a pharmacy is contrary to the fundamentals of the
practice of pharmacy and the potential for
harm to flow is high.
respect to paragraph 120(e)(i) of the Respondent’s submissions the
Tribunal accepts this is the practice the Respondent
says is in place in the
pharmacy. Evidence was heard from Mr Mahony to the effect that regular checks of
the expiry date of medications
in the dispensary were an integral part of
professional practice. The system described by Mr Mahony involved the
identification and
placement of stock with a short expiry date so that it will
be dispensed first. Further, as a part of this regular checking process
stock that may have gone out of date (or only having a short time to expiry)
without being dispensed could be identified and
disposed of appropriately.
Tribunal notes the contents of paragraphs 121, 122 and 123 of the
Respondent’s submissions. The Tribunal notes that the
Respondent’s
evidence was that he has a poor history of complying with his medication
regimen, extending over a significant
period of time, not limited to the time he
was brought to the attention of the Health Department as recorded in the report
by Dr Klug. The symptoms of tiredness and lethargy the Respondent
reported to Dr Klug as being a result of his lack of compliance
medication regimen was consistent with the Respondent’s appearance
throughout the Inquiry. The Tribunal accepts Dr
Klug’s observation that
the rate at which the Respondent was receiving his testosterone injections could
be validated by way
of his treating GP’s notes. No such notes were
produced to the Tribunal. Further, the Tribunal notes the evidence of the
that while the testosterone injections were prescribed by the GP he
did not necessarily have to attend the GP on each occasion the
injections were
administered. Rather, he could administered them to himself or may seek
assistance, for instance by his wife, with
the injection without attending the
doctor’s surgery.

of knowledge regarding the keeping of the drug keys on the person of the

Respondent now takes care to ensure all appropriate items have an AustR or an
AustL number. While the Tribunal can accept
that checking for such markings
would not be usually undertaken with respect to stock arriving from a

this should not be applied to stock purchased from a
‘Chinese shop’
. People purchasing products from pharmacies
are relying upon the expertise of the registered pharmacist. They are entitled
so. By his lack of attention to these details he placed the public at

that there are
‘50 to 100 pharmacies’
in Sydney that would not comply with
the requirement to only stock retail therapeutic goods bearing AustR and AustL
numbers, this
