Tribunal notes the submission made in paragraph 32 of the Respondent’s
Supplementary Submissions.
Tribunal notes that the Respondent’s Supplementary Submissions do not
address the issue of the conduct detailed in Particular
6 of the Complaint.
Although there was no finding of unsatisfactory professional conduct made with
respect to this Particular there
was a finding that the conduct fell below the
acceptable standard. Conduct of this type was detected by the April Audit as the
dispensed on 17 January 2011 the drug dexamphetamine on the second
repeat of a non-conforming prescription dated 2 August 2010. While
the initial
dispensing was permissible under Clause 38 of the
Poisons and Therapeutic
Goods Regulation 2002
any subsequent dispensing required an amended or new
prescription.
this point the Tribunal refers back to the evidence of the Respondent that as at
April 2011 he did not fully appreciate his obligations
under Clause 112 of the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods Regulations noting that this position is despite
all of the interventions that
had taken place by that time attempting to address
deficits in his professional practice.
Tribunal notes the significant impact the Respondent’s medical condition
has had on the conduct subject of the Complaint.
The Respondent has not had
evidence of a medically supported treatment plan for his condition or any system
to ensure compliance
therewith. Had the Respondent chosen to appropriately treat
his condition he may not have suffered from the symptoms and illnesses
attributes to his failure to comply with treatment. The Tribunal may have
weighed this factor heavily had this been the first
occasion when the Respondent
fell ill, particularly if he was unaware of the causation of the symptoms and
repeated illness. However,
in the circumstances of this matter it cannot be
accorded significant weight in view of the Respondent’s knowledge of his
condition, the three sets of convictions and the previous disciplinary
proceedings.
Tribunal does not accept that the Respondent’s conduct was careless. He
failed to attend to a number of the statutory requirements
relating to Schedule
8 drugs over a significant period of time. It is not a case of just missing one
count of one drug inadvertently.
It is not carless conduct to put a rubber band
around the returned boxes rather than take the time to properly identify this
The Respondent could have spent time on statutory compliance rather than
making the Alphapharm entries. He could have employed additional
staff to take
some of the workload so he could attend to statutory compliance tasks. At this
point the Tribunal notes the oral evidence
of the Respondent around transcript
page 73 that he had noticed that the returned Endone® tablets had been
‘inadvertently’
amalgamated with the stock Endone® and that
despite this he had not taken steps to separate them or inform the other
pharmacists
of this occurrence. Rather the Respondent stated he was waiting for
the Pharmacy Council Inspector to attend to destroy the tablets.
The Tribunal is
of the view that this is an example of evidence that the Respondent has not
acted in a professionally appropriate
manner. The alternate view is that the
evidence of the Respondent is just not credible.
Tribunal is also troubled about the lack of clarity regarding the supervision of
the Respondent’s practice in the first
two weeks of April 2011. In the
circumstances of this matter, when operating under an undertaking made to the
Supreme Court, there
should be no lack of clarity as to when the Respondent was
permitted to work without supervision.
in some respects the use of the term ‘Business Plan’ may not have
been of great assistance to the Respondent, what
must have been clear to the
Respondent from the time the first Tribunal handed down its decision was that
there were serious concerns
regarding his professional practice and the criminal
offences he had committed while conducting that practice. While the Tribunal
accepts that Mr Mahony had set a timetable to work towards as part of the
Business Plan/mentoring arrangement, this cannot release
the Respondent from the
obligations placed upon him as a registered health practitioner. The nature of
the defects in the Respondent’s
practice identified by the PSB 15 April
audit are significant and in the context of this Respondent’s practice and
circumstances
must be, in the Tribunal’s view, carefully considered. When
regard is had to the circumstances of the Respondent at the time
of the 15 April
Audit the Tribunal concludes that the Respondent has not overcome the deficits
(from the misconduct finding) in his
practice to a satisfactory point, nor have
his actions allowed the Tribunal to repose confidence in his current fitness to
(after the finding of unfitness based on convictions).
Tribunal has considerable evidence before it that the Respondent had been
informed of the serious position his registration was
in. Mr Mahony’s
dedication to supporting the Respondent to overcome the deficits in his practice
is evidence not only in the
dedication that was obvious from his oral evidence
before the Tribunal but also evident from comments such as that at the end of
the email he sent to the Respondent on 24 November 2010 (as set out above in the
section dealing with the evidence of Mr Mahony).
While the Tribunal has
considered the conduct of the Respondent within the context of this matter, the
Tribunal has not held him
to some higher standard. The Respondent’s
conduct is properly adjudged having regard to his training and experience and to
the circumstances of the matters before the Tribunal.
Tribunal makes reference to Mr Mahony’s view that, in essence, the
Pharmacy Council did not make adequate provision for
the procedure when there
was a hand over from a supervising pharmacist to the Respondent (see detailed
above in the summary of Mr
Mahony’s evidence). While the Tribunal accepts
it would have been put beyond any doubt had such a matter been spelt out in
form of documentation, that this is a matter that is fundamental to the practice
of pharmacy. While it may be that the name
‘Business Plan’ may have
the unfortunate consequence of some professional practice issues being
overlooked, this is not
a matter that can be laid at the feet of the Council or
indeed the independent auditor, Mr Piggott. All registered pharmacists have
obligation to ensure that matters are in order when they take over
responsibility for a pharmacy. There are also responsibilities
that fall to the
owners of pharmacies that cannot be delegated.
Tribunal accepts the comments made by Mr Mahony in his report (see page 14 of
his report dated 9 May 2011) regarding the difficulties
faced by pharmacists
when presented with an inadequate or invalid script. The Tribunal notes Mr
Mahony’s view that the bona-fide
of the client and the prescribers’
intentions become the pharmacists’ responsibility. In the view of the
Tribunal it
is the pharmacists’ responsibility to dispense in accordance
with valid prescriptions and to be aware of the other professional
obligations
regarding appropriate dispensing which may involve the overriding of the
validity of the prescription.
Tribunal has set out the initial comments made by Mr Mahony in his Mentor Report
dated 27 June 2011 above. It is unfortunate that
there was a perception the
undertaking made by the Respondent to the Supreme Court was anything other than
an interim position, made
pending the redetermination of the matter by the
Tribunal as remitted by the Court. This misconception did not assist.
Tribunal notes that the Respondent did not provide evidence from the pharmacist
working in the pharmacy on 12 April 2011 that
they did not change the
‘default’ setting that recorded the Respondent as the dispensing
pharmacist or that they dispensed
the medication on 12 April 2011.
there is a natural sympathy for an aversion to injections, the consequences of
the Respondent’s avoidance of his medication
have been immense. There is
an obligation on all professional persons to work within their limitations. This
issue is discussed above.
The Respondent’s failure to comply with
treatment demonstrates a lack of judgment on his part as he has not taken
steps to keep himself well by managing his illness.
the Tribunal is of the view that the deficits in the Respondent’s
knowledge remain at a significant level, a level incompatible
with maintaining
registration, even with stringent conditions. The Tribunal also finds that the
Respondent has insurmountable issues
with the retention of knowledge and the
application of knowledge to his practice.
Tribunal accepts the submission of the HCCC (at paragraph 22) that conditions
have already been placed on the Respondent’s
registration in the past and
the change in the practises of the Respondent were not what would be expected of
a registered pharmacist.
Further, it falls well below what should be expected
even of a junior pharmacist let alone a senior practitioner with the
Respondent’s
experience. The Tribunal is of the view that the Respondent
does not demonstrate sufficient aptitude to take on board any significant
benefit from the assistance he has been given nor any initiative to instigate
change for himself. With so many examples in evidence
of situations where the
Respondent stated he would only take action if a matter was brought to his
attention and other examples of
where he still took no action even when he was
aware of a matter the Tribunal has formed the view that overall the
Respondent’s
conduct is strongly indicative of a very reactive nature
rather than a proactive nature. The re-design of the dispensary suggested
in 2001 by supervisors appointed by the Board were not in fact undertaken until
February 2011 (with the intervention of the
Undertaking to the Supreme Court and
the guidance of Mr Mahony) when, in effect, his hand was absolutely forced.
Further, the Respondent
had not even taken steps such as remedying the deficits
in his knowledge that were evident from the offending conduct such as what
difference between an AustL and an AustR number. The Tribunal accepts the
submission made by the HCCC (at paragraph 30) that
the Respondent is dependent
on others, shows little or no initiative or even where to start to address
defects in his practice.
Tribunal has taken into account steps the Respondent has taken to improve
matters, substantially with Mr Mahony admirably being
the driving force.
However, the Tribunal is substantially concerned about the ethical and proper
practising of the Respondent rather
than the appearance of the pharmacy.
Tribunal notes that consideration of whether a Respondent has been able to in
any adequate sense learn lessons from their earlier
misconduct was made by
Phegan J in the matter of
Theodorou v Dean
authority provided to the Tribunal by counsel for the HCCC. This was an appeal
from the decision of the Board –
Dean v Theodorou
[2002] NSWPB 5.
Tribunal also notes that an earlier decision made by the Board with respect to
the same respondent, being
Ian Dean v Theodoros Theodorou,
Pharmacy Board
of NSW (10 July 1996) was provided to the Tribunal by the Respondent’s
Counsel. The Tribunal is of the view
that the Respondent has demonstrated
extremely limited aptitude to learn lessons from his prior misconduct and
limited aptitude to
learn from other registered pharmacists. The Tribunal notes
that the Respondent has a number of convictions in addition to the disciplinary
processes detailed herein.
the context of the Respondent’s disciplinary history the Tribunal is
particularly concerned with the areas of non-compliance
identified by the
inspection undertaken by the Pharmaceutical Services Unit on 15 April 2011 (see
summary at paragraph 31(iii) of
the HCCC Submissions).
the Tribunal notes the opinion expressed by Mr Mahony that Mr Lee was
responsible for making the correct endorsement in the
Drug Register for the
dispensing of Patient E’s OxyContin® medication on 25 March 2011 when
the Respondent was not working
under the supervision of Mr Ho. The Tribunal
notes the dispensing label records the Respondent as the dispensing pharmacist.
presence of the Respondent’s name on the label places the legal
responsibility for the dispensing of the medication and the
notation in the
Schedule 8 Register with the Respondent. If the Respondent was in fact only
attending to the ‘retail side’
of the pharmacy at that time then he
should have ensured that the settings on the computer did not
‘automatically’ print
out his name onto the label.
Respondent’s evidence regarding the placement of an elastic band around
the boxes containing 160 Endone® tablets (that
had been returned and
therefore it would have been illegal to dispense) is indicative of the
ineffectual manner in which the Respondent
addresses matters of professional
significance. Although there were only a small number of pharmacists dispensing
in the pharmacy
it was the evidence of the Respondent that there was nothing
other than the elastic band to indicate these were returned stock and
should not be re-dispensed but rather were awaiting destruction.
the qualifications placed upon his evidence under cross-examination the Tribunal
has taken a cautious approach to the evidence
of Dr Klug. Dr Klug was placed in
the difficult position of not being fully appraised of the factual basis of the
matters under consideration.
One matter that was able to be distilled from Dr
Klug’s evidence was the potential difficulties (‘risk of psychiatric
illness’ and otherwise) that would be faced by a person suffering from
hypopituitarism if the condition was not appropriately
treated. He expressed a
view that care should be provided by an endocrinologist. In the circumstances
where the Respondent is a qualified
health practitioner and has suffered from
this condition for many years it is of grave concern that he does not have such
arrangements
in place. Rather, the Respondent’s evidence is that he avoids
the medication prescribed by his general practitioner as he has
an aversion to
needles. Further, it is his evidence that his general energy levels are improved
if he does comply with his medication
level of supervision he has received is more than that received by a new
registrant and is not compatible with the practice of
an experienced
professional.
Tribunal is left with a picture of recalcitrant behaviour on the part of the
Respondent. There have been steps towards remediation
in the last 12 months.
These must be viewed in the context of their degree and reactive (rather than
proactive) nature. The Tribunal
is left in the position where it cannot be
reasonably satisfied that there are any set of conditions that will ensure the
safety is maintained.
is also the view of the Tribunal that the protection of the reputation of the
profession is another factor that leads to the conclusion
that the appropriate
order is one of de-registration. Not only would members of the public have an
expectation that registered pharmacists
would comply with legislative
requirements relating to the handling of medications, other members of the
profession also, rightly
so, have the expectation that legislation will be
complied with.
would be no deterrent effective if a practitioner who had breached relevant
legislation on so many occasions over such a length
of time was allowed to
continue to practice.
only is it the view of the Tribunal that de-registration is required to fulfil
all of the well-defined elements of the role of
the Tribunal in a protective
circumstances of the convictions
would also result in an order for
de-registration.
the Tribunal has formed the view that the Respondent is unlikely to be fit to
that he may apply himself in a more
effective manner to the myriad of ways he has already intensively been subjected
to with the
significant support of others. The Tribunal is left with
reservations that the Respondent will be able to remedy the defects he currently
displays in the period of 18 months suggested by the HCCC in its written
Submissions as an appropriate minimum period to fix for
review of the
de-registration order. The Tribunal will make the order in the terms submitted
by the HCCC so as to leave open the
possibility that the Respondent
rehabilitates without having issues with respect to recency of practice
requirements under the
National Law.
Tribunal accepts the evidence of Mr Piggott. While the business plan was
approved by Mr Piggott on 3 April 2011, the Tribunal
has already noted that the
business plan is not seen by the Tribunal as the panacea for the deficits in the
Respondent’s practice
as identified by the findings made with respect to
the Particulars of Complaint. While the Business Plan developed by Mr Mahony may
‘bear fruits’ over the coming years in a business sense, this
Tribunal is responsible for the public safety, the maintenances
reputation of the profession and the confidence of the public in the profession
at the present time. The Tribunal does not
have a role in the profitability or
otherwise of a pharmacy per se. It does have a role in the regulation of the
members of the profession.
Tribunal accepts that having a properly functioning pharmacy would facilitate
the Respondent’s compliance with all relevant
legislative and regulatory
provisions rather than it ‘ensuring’ such compliance. The Tribunal
notes the submission that
the Respondent is committed to such a course (i.e.
having a properly functioning pharmacy). What is missing from this equation is
‘follow through’ on the commitment by way of self-initiated steps
and, more fundamentally, the remedying of the deficits
in his knowledge, skill
and judgment regarding the practice of pharmacy. The Respondent has had many
years to have a properly functioning
pharmacy. He has had significant assistance
from other experienced pharmacists to achieve this goal. The Respondent has now
had significant
assistance in trying to address the deficits in his practice.
The efforts of Mr Dash were admirable but in light of the Respondent’s
subsequent conduct the teaching appears to have failed to resonate with the
Respondent. It is unfortunate that the Respondent has
not had been able to
transpose the ‘learning of the rules’ he gained from Mr Dash into
clinical reasoning and decision
Tribunal notes that while relying upon occasional ill health (having the flu) as
part of his ‘defence’, the Respondent
made known to the Tribunal his
long term health issue substantially through the evidence of Dr Klug. It is of
concern that the Respondent
has not adopted an appropriate treatment regimen
despite his health issue having the potential to seriously impact upon his
to practise. While at one level an aversion to receiving injections is
understandable, if the results of failure to comply with medication
prescribed is seriously impacting on the performance of your professional role
it becomes inexcusable. The Tribunal does not have
evidence that the Respondent
is currently complying with an appropriate medication/treatment regimen. The
explanation of being ‘overworked’
provided by the Respondent to Dr
Klug is not an acceptable excuse for failure to comply with legislative
requirements thereby putting
public safety at risk. The Respondent’s
position was not helped by failing to provide Dr Klug with a complete forensic
the Tribunal accepted the Respondent’s submissions (at paragraph 120(a))
that he will be more
pharmaceutical products not bearing an Aust R or an Aust L number the Tribunal
cannot be reasonably satisfied
that the Respondent will maintain this level of
care for any period of time. The past conduct of the Respondent is demonstrative
of repeated failure to maintain compliance with statutory requirements or good
practises. Despite the Respondent’s evidence
that he had not
‘completed the same wrongdoings’ (T113) the evidence contains
examples of numerous occasions on which
the Respondent has breached legislative
requirements placed around the dispensing and handling of medications.
view expressed by Dr Klug that the failure to take hormone replacement therapy
would give rise to a risk of psychiatric illness,
particularly depression (see
paragraph 123 of the Respondent’s submissions) is of particular concern to
the Tribunal given
the evidence of the Respondent that he has not consistently
complied with the regimen for quite some time. The Tribunal accepts the
Dr Klug that it would be preferable for the Respondent to come under the care of
en endocrinologist. This should have been
obvious to the Respondent for many
years given his illness commenced in childhood and given the stated impact it
has had on his health
and professional practice.
there is no doubt that the Respondent’s medical condition has impacted
upon his health significantly, it is not a matter
that arose by surprise and
without warning. While his health may be part of the explanation for the conduct
of the Respondent, it
certainly cannot, in the Tribunal’s view, be an
excuse. It is a ‘self-induced’ explanation in as much as it was
predictable result of the Respondent’s failure to comply with his
treatment regimen. The Respondent had a known and accepted
treatment option open
to him. He failed to comply with the treatment option. It is a factor within the
direct control of the Respondent.
There is an obligation placed on all health
practitioners to ensure that they are fit to work. Impairments (such as drug or
addiction/abuse or long term illness) that impact upon the
practitioner’s ability to work as a health practitioner should be
to the Council so that appropriate assessment can be made. Section 140 of the
National Law (as it applies in every jurisdiction
apart from Western Australia)
provides for the mandatory reporting of impairment that places the public at
substantial risk of harm.
100% commitment from the Respondent to be a good pharmacist and to meet all of
his professional obligations should have come after
his first criminal
conviction (paragraph 128 Respondent’s submissions). Rather, it has come
after three sets of criminal convictions
and two instances of disciplinary
findings of professional misconduct and a partially successful appeal to the
Supreme Court which
afforded him a last minute reprieve from a forced sale of
his pharmacy. In the circumstances of this matter (repeated undertakings
comply in the future, failure to address the underlying medical issue etc) the
Tribunal is not in a position to be reasonably
satisfied that the Respondent
will follow through with his verbal commitment to Mr Mahony. The driving force
of Mr Mahony can also
be seen from the contents of the Respondent’s
submissions at paragraphs 135 to 140. While it is admirable, it does not
in the Tribunal a sense that the Respondent is able to achieve an
appropriate level of practice without considerable outside input
of many kinds.
There is little to assure the Tribunal that there is any internally driven
desire and/or capacity for change on the
part of the Respondent. Although he has
been co-operative with those involved with the current process of supervision
the Respondent
shows no signs of taking ownership of his rehabilitation.
Tribunal acknowledges the changes made by the Respondent to his practice as a
result of the commitment made by the Respondent
to Mr Mahony in May 2011
(paragraph 129 – 134 of the Respondent’s submissions). The Tribunal
notes that Mr Dash met with
the Respondent on four occasions, including a two
hour tuition session, two visits to the pharmacy, and one meeting for the
of a one and a quarter hour examination. Mr Dash reported copies of the
relevant legislation were not located in the pharmacy on
the occasion of his
visit in June 2011 contrary to the requirements of the legislation. Mr Dash is
eminently qualified to provide
tuition on the topic of statutory compliance in
the practice of pharmacy. The Tribunal had the opportunity of perusing the
examination
paper of the Respondent and does not doubt his knowledge has
improved with respect to the specific matters subject of the two hours
tuition provided. However, given the range of deficits in the Respondent’s
knowledge it is not accepted that those deficits
have now been remedied to a
point where the Tribunal can be satisfied that the public safety will be
as undertaking additional training was an appropriate step for the Respondent,
the process of undertaking the QCPP process (referred
to in paragraphs 137 and
138 of the Respondent’s submissions) is viewed by the Tribunal as
appropriate, meritorious and valuable.
Once again Mr Mahony appears from the
evidence to be the driving force for this process.
indicated above the Tribunal notes the submission made at paragraph 141 of the
Respondent’s submissions that Mr Stephen Cohen
agreed in late June 2011 to
supervise the Respondent in the performance of his professional duties as a
pharmacist for six hours
per day for two days per week. It is unfortunate that
the oral evidence of Mr Cohen revealed that he did not know whether or not
Pharmacy Board of Australia existed and could not give an account of the
statutory obligations surrounding the term
‘Pharmacist in
. While the regular reports made by Mr Cohen are informal and
brief, one of the reports provided by Mr Cohen indicates that he may
appreciate the role of state legislation in determining the requirements for
endorsement of intervals of dispensing as contact
was made with Medicare to
clarify an issue of this nature (Medicare being a federal body not responsible
for determining the endorsement
of intervals of dispensing on prescriptions
which is covered by state legislation). The Tribunal notes that these matters
matters subject of the current Inquiry and has only made mention of them
due to the Respondent’s reliance on Mr Cohen as a
registered pharmacist
with 40 years’ experience and he proffers his opinion of the
Respondent’s current practices for
the Tribunal’s consideration.
While a lack of detailed knowledge of the regulatory scheme that came into place
for ten health
professions on 1 July 2010 may have been understandable at an
earlier time, as all practitioners of these ten professions have now
re-registration on at least one occasion since the introduction of the scheme,
knowledge of the existence of the relevant
Board is expected.
of the matters reported by Mr Cohen (see paragraph 142 of the Respondent’s
submissions) have been observed by other registered
pharmacists in the past.
Subsequently they have been found to be still lacking. While the Tribunal
accepts the observations of Mr
Cohen, there is nothing that assists the Tribunal
to accept that this situation will be maintained by the Respondent of his own
Ultimately,
the Tribunal takes the view that little can turn on the view expressed by Mr
Cohen that the Respondent is fit to practise
pharmacy and is not a risk for the
public, both for the reasons set out above and on the basis that this is a
question for the Tribunal
to determine based on the totality of the evidence.
Tribunal accepts the positive comments made by Mr Piggott in his report dated 22
July 2011 regarding the recent observation made
of the Respondent’s

Respondent (see paragraph 144
of the Respondent’s submissions). The
Tribunal notes that the matters reported upon by Mr Piggott are elementary and
fundamental
to a pharmacy practice. The Tribunal again has concerns regarding
the Respondent’s ability to maintain even these perfunctionary
With respect to the submission made in paragraph 145 of the Respondent’s
submissions the Tribunal notes that not only
did Mr Piggott state that he would
give the keys of his pharmacy to the Respondent to allow him to work there but
added that he would
keep a close eye on the Respondent as he did with all of his
employees. Once again it is the Tribunal’s view that it is a matter
determination by the Tribunal based upon all of the evidence.
respect to the submission made by the Respondent at paragraph 146 the Tribunal
notes that qualifications are only one element
of the requirements for
registration.
is of concern to the Tribunal regarding the history of convictions set out in
the Respondent’s submissions (see paragraph
149) is that the Respondent
has not demonstrated that he has made significant improvements in his
understanding of professional responsibilities
or an improved ability to act
upon the purported increase in his knowledge. When his practice is put under
close scrutiny departure
from statutory obligations are identified time and time
again. The Tribunal notes the Respondent’s submissions as to the effluxion
of time since these convictions. The mere effluxion of time does not, in itself,
remedy defects in professional conduct nor make
it more appropriate for a person
to remain in a profession with the weight of the circumstances of such
convictions.
Tribunal was assisted by the report of Dr Klug. While Dr Klug was not apprised
of all relevant facts and circumstances he provided
guidance as to the nature of
untreated panhypopituitarism. The Tribunal is not of the view that the lethargy
flowing from the Respondent’s
failure to appropriately treat this
condition is a mitigating factor in the circumstances. The Respondent was first
diagnosed with
this condition at age 14. On his own accounts he has always been
aware of the consequences of not treating this condition. The Respondent
also cognisant of the fact that the symptoms that flowed from a failure to
maintain his treatment regimen were detrimental to
his performance of his duties
as a pharmacist. No benefit can flow to the Respondent from his own failure to
care for his health.
Tribunal has had regard to the two character references provided. The Tribunal
is of the opinion that they are more in the nature
of a view of the
Respondent’s reputation rather than character. It does not appear that
either of the referees was provided
with a full account of the
Respondent’s criminal record. This is a matter that impacts upon the
weight that can be placed upon
the references. The Tribunal notes that both
referees appear to be people of good standing within the community. The Tribunal
no doubt that the Respondent is pleasant and convivial with his patients.
Although this is to be expected of all registered pharmacists
this is not the
issue being considered by the Tribunal and does not overcome the issues of a
fundamental nature identified by the
findings made against the Respondent.
to the professional reference in evidence from Mr Ho, the Tribunal notes that he
was only briefed by the Respondent regarding the
findings of professional
misconduct. He stated that he did not receive any information from the Council
as to how supervision should
be undertaken.
Tribunal has had the benefit of additional evidence. The Tribunal did not
receive a report from Mr Cox and only knows of his
views as reported by Mr
Mahony. Mr Piggott has a limited role in the matter and expressed an overall
view that the Respondent did
not have a free flowing ability to comply with
legislative requirements. Mr Dash also had a limited role in this matter, both
terms of time and purpose, although his finding that the relevant legislation
was missing from the pharmacy at a time that the Tribunal
was part heard is of
concern. In early 2011 Mr Mahony wrote a positive report regarding the
Respondent’s practice and then
the 15 April Audit undertaken by the PSU
identified significant defects. Mr Mahony then adjusted his view (appropriately)
he considered the Respondent to be a ‘work in progress’,
not able to be finally assessed until around the end of 2012
completion of the business plan).
respect to the submission that the 15 April Audit undertaken by the PSU proved
that the journey to complete reformation is not
complete (paragraph 153 of the
Respondent’s submissions), the Tribunal is mindful that what is being
determined is current
fitness, not how the Respondent might be presenting in 12
Mahony gave evidence that he had initiated the Respondent adopting the MYOB
computer system. The Respondent’s evidence was
that he had purchased it
but had not installed it. The Respondent was unable to produce staff rosters and
payroll excel spread sheets
could not be printed out. Once again the inertia
demonstrated by the Respondent is considerable.
noted above, the Tribunal does not view the conduct of the Respondent with
respect to the Alphapharm entries to be careless (as
submitted in paragraph 154)
or isolated (as submitted in paragraph 156). The sheer number of entries and the
purpose for which the
Respondent made the entries indicates the conduct is
deliberate and calculated in nature. The entries were made over a two day period
(one day in each of two consecutive months). This conduct was not undertaken for
a legitimate purpose. Other conduct of the Respondent
may be characterised as
reckless, as it was undertaken in ignorance of matters that are fundamental to
the practice of pharmacy.
The past conduct of the Respondent, such as requesting
prescriptions for doctors for medication dispensed, is another example of
conduct that is far from proper.
the Tribunal accepts that it is not inevitable that the mentoring program
‘must inevitably fail’ (see the submission
in paragraph 157), there
is insufficient evidence to allow the Tribunal to conclude that it will
inevitably succeed. There is little
the Tribunal can find to provide any degree
of comfort that the Respondent is making progress at a sufficient rate and that
any progress
made will be permanent. Indeed, it is unfortunate that the Tribunal
finds that the evidence is to the contrary. Advances made with
the significant
support of senior members of the profession are easily lost once the direct
supervision is not actively present.
Advances in knowledge are not applied in
his practice. His skill and care in practice, while moving in a positive
direction, have
still a long way to progress before meeting the appropriate
Tribunal has had regard to the submissions made on behalf of the Respondent
regarding the type of conditions that may be appropriate
in this matter (see
paragraphs 158, 159 and 160). The Tribunal accepts the view of Mr Piggott that
there was little else Mr Mahony
could do with respect to the development of the
business plan. However, given the repeated nature of the Respondent’s
in spite of the enormous amount of intervention in his practice the
Tribunal cannot be satisfied that complete remediation is possible
particularly
as the Respondent has not taken steps to increase his potential to meet all
professional obligations by ensuring his
health is optimal.
Respondent has stated that he is willing to change and comply with all
professional obligations. He has also stated that he is
remorseful for his
conduct (see paragraphs 162 to 164 of the Respondent’s submissions).
Remorse has not prevented him from
committing criminal offences on two occasions
after his first offence. His remorse and stated willingness to change has not
translated into tangible steps such as ensuring the pharmacy complies with
all requirements at all times, applying Mr Mahony’s
suggested improvements
promptly or complying with his own medication regimen to improve his chances of
not falling into the same
traps he has encountered in the past.
Tribunal is mindful of the observations made by Adams J that the initial
Tribunal’s finding of lack of remorse was misconceived.
This Tribunal has
the benefit of the additional evidence regarding the Respondent’s
continued failures to ensure his practice
complies with applicable statutory
provisions in addition to the evidence regarding his failure to comply with his
medication regimen.
While this Tribunal would not discount the importance of
running a pharmacy with appropriate levels of cleanliness and order given
function performed therein, this is not a matter that this Tribunal has had
regard to in forming the view that the remorse stated
by the Respondent has not
successfully translated across to changes in behaviour. Similarly the Tribunal
has not considered in any
way the rejection of the initial Tribunal of the
evidence of the Respondent regarding telephoning Dr Jackson.
Tribunal accepts that the Respondent is ashamed of his criminal convictions and
is remorseful for the predicament he is in where
his registration is in peril.
While the Tribunal accepts without reservation that this is what the Respondent
has said to Mr Mahony,
it is the actions of the Respondent that do not support
his statements of remorse as being able to provide comfort to the Tribunal
significant and sustained change is possible.
Tribunal accepts the submission made on behalf of the Respondent that he has
endeavoured over the last seven years to ‘rigorously’
observe his
obligations as a pharmacist, expressed to the Tribunal in March 2010 willingness
to participate in the supervision/mentoring
program and has been fully committed
to that program since November 2010 (see paragraph 170 of Respondent’s
submissions). The
Tribunal further accepts that the pharmacy has now been
transformed from the condition it was in when Mr Mahony undertook his initial
inspection and that further improvements are planned. The Tribunal notes that
earlier ‘transformations’ were achieved
with the assistance of the
supervisors put in place by the Orders made by the Board Inquiry.
rejecting the opinion proffered by Mr Mahony as to these improvements (see
paragraph 171 of the Respondent’s submissions),
as clearly they are
genuinely and sincerely held, the Tribunal is not able to be satisfied that the
progress has been sufficient
to address the deficits identified in the
Respondent’s practice to a position where conditions on registration would
the Tribunals obligation of ensuring public safety.
the Tribunal accepts that the Respondent has made a number of statements
regarding his commitment to rehabilitation since the
initial Tribunal’s
decision, in addition to participating in the mentoring program with Mr Mahony,
the Tribunal notes the difficulties
presented by the substantial findings as
well as subsequent events such as the 15 April audit conducted by the PSU
(paragraph 172
of the Respondent’s submissions).
Tribunal does not accept the submission made at paragraph 173 of the
Respondent’s submission. While it may be that the Respondent
‘his pharmacy practice to the required level’ the Tribunal cannot be
so satisfied on the evidence before it. This
is so despite acknowledging that
there has been significant support provided by Mr Mahony (a senior pharmacist of
good standing in
the profession) and that the Respondent has, on advice, taken
steps to initiate further improvements.
the views of witnesses such as Mr Mahony is considered in light of their
professional qualifications and experience, they are
not witnesses that should
be considered as ‘expert’ or ‘peer review’. In any case
this is a ‘peer’
tribunal and is well equipped to understand and
assess the evidence placed before it (refer to paragraph 175 of the
Respondent’s
submissions).
Tribunal is not persuaded by the submissions made on behalf of the Respondent in
paragraphs 176, 177 and 178 to the effect that
the Tribunal should consider a
further period of supervised practice to be potentially effective despite the
three month period of
supervision he undertook as a result of the Order of the
Board Inquiry (made on 14 February 2001). The basis for the Tribunal not
persuaded by the Respondent’s submission is that he has now had a period
of supervised practice since the undertaking
to the Supreme Court longer than
three months. The 2001 supervisor identified the issues either subject of the
Particulars of Complaint
or identified by Mr Mahony and took steps to address
them. There is evidence that there was a degree of planning and goal setting
undertaken by the supervisors and that there was communication between them. The
evidence before the Tribunal does not support a
finding that the supervision was
‘inconsistent, unfocussed and unsustained’. Rather, the evidence
supports there being
full time supervision by a registered pharmacist for the
three month period. There is no evidence of any ‘follow-up’
the three months of supervision initiated by any party – the Board, any of
the supervisors or the Respondent. While the
2001 supervision does not appear to
have had an ‘independent audit’ as part of the process, the nature
of the audit placed
before this Tribunal was of a limited nature as set out
above. The teasing out of the process of the handling of Schedule 8 drugs
matter not specifically listed as part of the supervision process is to ignore
the content of the reports made by the supervisors
and to artificially separate
this responsibility from the phrase ‘every aspect of the operations
of’ of the pharmacy.
The final matter submitted by the Respondent as
relevant to persuading the Tribunal to disregard the outcome of the 2001
supervision
was the attention of the supervisors to physical aspects of the
pharmacy such as layout, tidiness and staffing levels. The Tribunal
accepts that
these were issues of significant concern to the supervisors. The Tribunal also
notes that these were matters assessed
by Mr Mahony in 2010 as being of high
priority. From the descriptions provided in the documentation in evidence these
issues were
glaring and grave. It is clearly unsafe for medications to be
prepared in an unclean environment or to invite customers into a cluttered
or to expect employees to work in an unsafe workplace. This submission also
ignores the significant comments made by the supervisors
regarding more
substantial issues. Ultimately, there is evidence that deficits in his practice
are still patent despite the different
style of supervision undertaken by the
Respondent since the Supreme Court undertaking.
Tribunal has dealt with submissions such as those set out in paragraph 179 in
addressing earlier submissions to the same effect.
Tribunal is also unable to accept the submissions made at paragraphs 180 and 181
of the Respondent’s submissions. While
the Tribunal acknowledges that the
Respondent has made statements regarding his commitment to change and received
advice from Mr
Mahony, Mr Dash and others it does not follow that these matters
will make the outcome of supervision any more successful than it
was in 2001.
The comments of magistrates on the occasion of his criminal convictions, the
Reasons document of the Board Inquiry and
the comments of the five supervisors
should have caused significant ‘moment’ for the Respondent. While
those who have
provided more recent advice are senior members of the profession,
there is little that differentiates the advice they have given
to the Respondent
from the advice provided by the five supervisors.
Tribunal has carefully considered potential orders that would allow the Tribunal
to be satisfied it has fulfilled its statutory
function of protecting the public
safety, including those suggested by Mr Piggott in his evidence as set out in
paragraph 184 and
185 of the Respondent’s submissions (leaving aside the
finding of unfitness based on the circumstances of the convictions).
Tribunal is not of the view that there are any set of conditions that could be
imposed at the current time that could allow the
Tribunal to be reasonably
satisfied that public safety was ensured. Essentially, the Tribunal is of the
view that the Respondent
is likely to be permanently unfit to practise as a
registered pharmacist. The Tribunal is therefore of the view that the
appropriate
order to make is de-registration. This order also, in the view of
the Tribunal, satisfies the role of the Tribunal to maintain public
in the profession of pharmacy, to act as a deterrent to other pharmacists from
engaging in repeated breaches of legislative
requirements and maintain the
reputation of the profession.
Tribunal has had regard to the submissions as to the applicability of principles
HCCC v Wingate [2007
] NSWSC 326,
Lucire V Health Care Complaints
Commission (No 2)
With respect to the first decision the
Tribunal has not formed the view that the need to impose conditions demonstrates
to practice. Quite clearly the National Law contemplates findings such
as professional misconduct resulting in the imposition of
conditions on
registration being imposed. The decision in Lucire relates to conditions imposed
after a finding of unsatisfactory
professional conduct.
Tribunal has also had regard to the submission made in paragraph 188 of the
Respondent’s submissions regarding the principle
Karalasingham
While parity is appropriate, each case must
turn on its own particular facts. The Respondent’s submissions provided a
analysis of a number of cases. The Tribunal has considered these
matters and cannot accept the submission made at paragraph 207 that
it would be
a matter of ‘principle, public interest and consistency’ to impose
conditions on the Respondent’s practice.
the Tribunal accepts the submission of the Respondent that the conduct subject
of the Particulars of Complaint covers the period
September 2003 to February
2005, it is the view of the Tribunal that the totality of the conduct of the
Respondent in the evidence
before the Tribunal must be considered in order that
appropriate orders are made.
Tribunal notes the positive comments made by Mr Mahony in his report dated 19
May 2010 (regarding his inspection of the Respondent’s
pharmacy on 17
February 2010). This evidence is compared and contrasted by the Tribunal with
the findings of the 15 April Audit undertaken
by the PSU. The Tribunal also
notes the oral evidence of Mr Mahony that he considers the situation to still be
‘work in progress’
with the outcome being ascertainable at
the end of the business plan.
Tribunal does not doubt the experience of Mr Mahony as noted by Adams J. It is
not the role of this Tribunal to comment upon the
adequacy of the business plan
subject of the undertaking to the Supreme Court. It does not appear to the
Tribunal that the business
plan was intended as a vehicle to rehabilitate the
Respondent. In many respects there was a lost opportunity for the Respondent to
prioritise professional standards (in particular legislative requirements
relating to the practice of pharmacy) and ethical matters.
J noted some of the matters Mr Mahony gave evidence about before the initial
Tribunal. This Tribunal had an opportunity to hear
additional evidence from Mr
Mahony, to undertake a more detailed questioning regarding his earlier evidence
and to consider Mr Mahony’s
evidence in the context of the additional
material. The Tribunal notes, as an example the evidence of Mr Mahony regarding
his encouragement
for the Respondent to adopt ‘good habits’. By this
Mr Mahony meant that there are certain matters that should just be
an integral
and fundamental part of the manner in which a registered pharmacist performs
their duties for which referral to textbooks,
policies and procedures and
legislation on each occasion is not required. It is the ‘good habit’
of the Respondent with
respect to compliance with legislative requirements of
the practice of pharmacy and overall professional conduct that is of concern
this Tribunal.
Ultimately,
the Tribunal has formed the view that although the Respondent has made some
steps towards improving his practice, much
of these can be characterised as
cosmetic in nature and not addressing the underlying issue of his lack of
knowledge, skill, judgment
and care in the practice of pharmacy, particularly
with respect to legislative requirements.
Respondent submitted that since the time of the judgment of Adams J he had taken
steps to comply with ‘relevant requirements
in respect to the matters the
subject of the complaint’ (paragraph 94). This Tribunal accepts that the
Respondent has taken
steps to comply with the specific subject matters of the
legislative breaches but the Respondent does not demonstrate compliance
legislative requirements as demonstrated by the 15 April Audit undertaken by the
PSU and other evidence such as the absence
of copies of relevant legislation in
the pharmacy noted by Mr Dash. The Tribunal also notes that many steps taken by
the Respondent
were a result of the assistance provided to him by Mr Mahony as a
result of the undertaking made to the Supreme Court.
Tribunal also notes the summary of discussions with the Respondent made by Mr
Mahony at page 9 of his February 2010 Peer Report.
At the particular point in
the comprehensive information gathering exercise Mr Mahony undertook in order to
write his report he records
having discussed the likely outcomes of ‘this
Board of Inquiry’ in light of his inability at that point to provide a
positive peer review. The observations of Mr Mahony are consistent with the
Tribunal’s observation that the Respondent has
not accorded his criminal
convictions or misconduct findings appropriate weight. The account of the
discussions provided by Mr Mahony
also illustrates the significant dedication
and detail of the counsel provided to the Respondent by Mr Mahony. The
alternatives set
out by Mr Mahony are in some respects prophetic and assure the
Tribunal that the Respondent was fully aware of the potential outcomes
making a ‘massive commitment to change his ways’. This section of Mr
Mahony’s report reads:
Having reached this point and convinced Mr Ng that I could not provide a
positive peer review in these circumstances, I discussed
with him the likely
outcomes of this Board of Inquiry.
Present his case without my review and with only the HCCC documentation. The
most likely outcome would be deregistration. Mr Ng
had failed to comprehend the
impact of this and the likelihood of its
occurrence.
Present a reasonable case and show remorse and he could be suspended for up
to 12 months. Unlike last time when this was a matter
“supervision”, he would not be able to even enter the pharmacy
whilst ever it was open. Mr Ng also failed to comprehend
the impact of this
Present a case including a plan as outlined below and make the massive
commitment to change his ways. The outcome may involve no
suspension but would
include considerable supervision and responsibility to the Board which would
enable him to remain as a pharmacist
and continue to serve the people of Ryde
and would enable him to see his daughter registered and practice alongside
Summary of findings re Particulars of Complaints One and Two
Tribunal notes that each of the Particulars of Complaint were established. It
appears the initial Tribunal found unsatisfactory
professional conduct with
respect to Particulars 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 9 and 10 and that the conduct constituted
professional misconduct.
This Tribunal made an additional finding of
unsatisfactory professional conduct with respect to Particular 8. Complaint Two
is established.
Summary of finding re Complaint Three
Tribunal notes the submissions of the parties that this is a matter for the
current Tribunal to consider as the matter was not
directed by the decision of
the Supreme Court.
Tribunal notes the finding of the initial Tribunal with respect to Complaint
This Tribunal has made its own consideration of this Complaint. The Tribunal is
not of the view that this is a matter where it is
appropriate for a peer
reviewer to provide an opinion.
Tribunal is of the view that the circumstances of the offences are such that the
Respondent is rendered unfit in the public interest
to remain registered. The
Tribunal is of the view that the actions of the Respondent have not remedied
this unfitness.
The Respondent working without supervision
the evidence before the Tribunal it appears that the last day on which Mr Ho
worked in the Respondent’s pharmacy was 29
March 2011. It is noted that
the Respondent was required to work with supervision until such time as
appropriate additional sales
assistant staff were employed. The additional staff
member commenced employment at the Respondent’s pharmacy on 19 April 2011.
In the period between the time Mr Ho left employment at the pharmacy and the
time of the PSU audit on 15 April 2011 the following
Quincy Lee (Wik Ho Lee) worked as the Pharmacist in Charge on 30 and 31 March
and 1 April 2011. It is unclear as to who worked
in the pharmacy on 2 April
2011. On 3 April 2011 the Respondent worked without a supervising pharmacist for
a period of three hours.
On 4 April Mr Lee was the Pharmacist in Charge. On 5
April the Respondent worked without supervision.
Between 6 to 9
April 2011 inclusive Mr Lee was the Pharmacist in Charge.
The Respondent
was on holidays from 9 to 14 April inclusive.
On 15 April the
Respondent was working with Mr Lee. The name Sidney Ho was initially on display
as the Pharmacist in Charge and later
this was changed to Mr Lee as the
Pharmacist in Charge and the Respondent as the pharmacist on duty.
Tribunal also notes the evidence of the Respondent in his statement dated 10 May
2011 put before the Tribunal as volume R2, exhibit
10 at paragraph 23 that the
Respondent claimed Mr Ho worked at the pharmacy until 15 April 2011 as his
‘on-site supervisor,
in accordance with the supervision programme’.
Tribunal also notes the oral evidence of the Respondent regarding the matter of
when Mr Ho ceased working at the pharmacy. Initially
the Respondent stated that
Mr Ho had worked up until 5 April 2011 (T117.8, 11/5/11). The following day,
under cross-examination the
Respondent gave evidence that he had checked his
records and found that the last day Mr Ho had worked at the pharmacy was in fact
29 March 2011 (T207.12, 12/5/11). The Respondent apologised for
‘inaccurate information provided due to the poor recollection
misinterpretation’.
1 April 2011 the records indicate that the Respondent dispensed 100
dexamphetamine tablets (5mg) to a patient. A copy of the repeat
authorisation
form is annexure NG-1 of volume R2 – exhibit 10 which has a duplicate
dispensing label affixed to it which records
the Respondent’s initials.
This indicates that the Respondent dispensed the medication. When the PSU
inspection occurred on
15 April 2011 a discrepancy in the 5mg dexamphetamine
tablets was noted and an entry was made to adjust the record accordingly.
Respondent also gave evidence that he worked on 16, 17 and 18 April 2011 without
Mr Ho present and prior to the time that the
additional shop assistant commenced
Tribunal notes the evidence of Dr Klug that the Respondent stated that he was
making every effort to control the business. This
contrasted with the evidence
of Mr Mahony that various matters found to be at issue by the 15 April Audit
were not the responsibility
of the Respondent. The Respondent made several
statements to Dr Klug regarding the role of his mentor and supervisor. He saw
mentor as improving his business and helping him offload his workload. When
the Respondent consulted Dr Klug on 27 April 2011 he
talked about his
apprehension regarding his future, his feeling of shame over being de-registered
(by the first Tribunal), of his
remorse for having allowed this situation to
have occurred, he accepted that he had done the wrong thing. The Respondent told
Klug that he was doing all that he could to control his business and he was
fully prepared to implement the supervision program and
business plan that had
been ‘dictated by the court’.
Summary of Findings
Tribunal made a finding of professional misconduct and it is upon this finding
that the Tribunal intends making Orders. On the
balance of the evidence before
the Tribunal, the Tribunal is reasonably satisfied that the Respondent is
currently permanently unfit
to practice pharmacy.
the Tribunal is of the view that the circumstances of the Respondent’s
convictions render him unfit in the public interest
to practice pharmacy and
that this unfitness remains current. Given the shift in position of the HCCC
with respect to whether a finding
of the Complaint of fitness on the basis of
the convictions is open to the Tribunal this finding will not form the basis for
Tribunal’s final Orders.
Additional evidence and Inquiry
reports were supplied to the Tribunal from Mr Cohen regarding his supervision of
the Respondent until the Tribunal ordered
that no further reports would be
received on 9 March 2012. It is difficult for the Tribunal to place significant
weight on the reports
of Mr Cohen given factors such as the limited time that he
had spent with the Respondent at the time he gave his oral evidence, his
familiarity with the relevant legislation and his limited role in the
Respondent’s pharmacy even without regard to
the glaring absence of any
mention in his Reports regarding the 8 September 2011 incident despite his
presence in the pharmacy at
the time of Ms Dolan’s attendance on 13
September 2011.
Respondent also sought to rely on a further report from Mr Mahony. The Tribunal
allowed the Respondent to rely on these additional
documents and for the HCCC to
provide a further report from Ms Dolan. The Tribunal re-convened for the purpose
of formally receiving
these documents and allowing cross-examination of these
witnesses. The Tribunal notes this accords with the Tribunal’s position
that all relevant material should be considered by the Tribunal (see T200).
noted above the Respondent gave further evidence when the Tribunal reconvened on
31 May 2012. The Tribunal accepts that the Respondent
believes his statements to
the effect that he has changed his ways. The Tribunal also accepts that the
Respondent believes he has
acted responsibly. However, the evidence before the
Tribunal does not allow the Tribunal to have confidence that when presented with
a new situation or even another example of a situation in which he has
previously acted erroneously that the Respondent will not
Tribunal also accepts the Respondent genuinely desires to remain in business as
a community pharmacist. However, the Tribunal
is of the view that the Respondent
lacks the skill, judgment, knowledge and care required to practice at the
standard required of
a registered pharmacist.
may well be that the Respondent will be better placed to remedy the defects in
his practice if he comprehensively addresses the
admitted deficits in the
treatment of his medical condition. The Tribunal hopes that the Respondent takes
heed of the evidence of
Dr Klug given the potentially serious ramifications of
not doing so.
would be difficult to fashion a more supportive framework than that which has
been put in place around the Respondent not just
over recent times but also in
the past as a result of the intervention of the Board Inquiry.
Respondent’s evidence to the effect that he had not considered the 8
September 2011 incident to be serious enough to discuss
it with Mr Mahony until
25 January 2012 (see paragraph 268 and T 571-573) is another example of the
Respondent’s lack of professional
judgment. By this point Mr Mahony had
provided the Respondent with an enormous amount of support and assistance. Mr
support of the Respondent is clearly evidence from the reports he
has provided and the evidence he has given before the Tribunal.
Respondent’s evidence that he was ‘
just waiting for the right
to inform Mr Mahony is not credible and the Tribunal notes
that by 25 January 2012 the Respondent would have been aware (on the assumption
that he would have needed to provide instructions re same) that the Tribunal was
holding a further Directions Hearing to discuss
the completion of the evidence
in this matter. Such conduct on the part of the Respondent does not bode well
for remediation occurring
within a short time frame or for there to be any
reliance on self-reporting by the Respondent. Another matter which adds to the
concern regarding the potential for remediation of the Respondent is
the lack of clarity around the issue of the motivation for the
Respondent’s repeated misconduct/breaches of professional conduct.
Tribunal cannot leave the issue of the 8 September 2011 incident without noting
the Respondent’s evidence that subsequent
to this incident being brought
to his attention that he had contacted the patient involved and been informed by
the patient that
he had not overdosed on the medication despite having received
information from an attending anaesthetist and registered nurse to
the contrary.
Other issues
Tribunal notes the evidence of the Respondent regarding his countermanding of
the advice provided on ‘Label 18’ which
relates to the interaction
between grapefruit and the particular medication. The Tribunal notes that the
Respondent explained his
failure to affix this warning label on the basis that
he had provided verbal advice to the patient regarding the intake of grapefruit
four hours before or after taking the medication. The Tribunal notes that the
advice provided in MIMS is to limit intake of grapefruit.
The advice provided by
the Respondent placed the patient potentially at risk of muscle and liver damage
and is considered by the
Tribunal to be advice below the standard reasonably
expected of any registered pharmacist.
Appropriate Orders
care has been taken by the Tribunal to ensure only matters relevant to the
Orders that should flow from the Findings made
are considered in the
determination of the Orders. This is not a matter where a clean, tidy and/or
financially viable pharmacy can
be determinative of the outcome. This Tribunal
is dealing with the registration of one pharmacist, the Respondent. Putting
the Finding that the circumstances of the convictions render the
Respondent currently unfit to practice, the Tribunal must be able
trust in the Respondent that he will practice in a manner that protects the
public safety, that maintains the reputation
of the profession and ensures the
public may repose confidence in the profession.
noted elsewhere the Tribunal considers the circumstances under which the
Respondent has practiced over recent times with the assistance
of supervisors,
tutors and mentors to have been supportive in the extreme. There is no doubt
that Mr Dash is eminent in his field,
that Mr Mahony is a senior experienced
member of the profession or that Mr Piggott did not undertake the task allocated
diligently.
Rather, the Tribunal finds that the Respondent is working to his
current capacity. While conceding some improvements have occurred
Respondent’s practise, there have not been, in the Tribunal’s view,
significant improvements in the Respondent’s
knowledge, skill, judgment or
is unfortunate that the practise of the Respondent has not reformed sufficiently
to allow the Tribunal to repose confidence in
his ability to practise safely.
The Tribunal notes that observations regarding the Respondent’s work ethic
and ethos made by
Mr Vincent, the HCCC peer reviewer and noted in the decision
of Adams J at paragraph 38.
misconduct is of a particularly serious nature. It relates to matters going to
the heart of the practise of pharmacy. It is conduct
that may have had some
explanation if the Respondent was poorly trained or inexperienced. There is no
evidence that the Respondent’s
training was deficient and he now has over
20 years’ experience as a community pharmacist in Australia. As noted
above significant
efforts have been made by a number of other members of the
profession to provide further training on a one-on-one basis with the
Respondent.
Respondent has demonstrated a high degree of recidivism in his conduct. The
submission made on his behalf that each of the acts
was ‘different’
(and therefore should not be looked at in some cumulative way) is not accepted
by the Tribunal. The conduct
mostly amounts to breaches of the legislation under
which this profession operates and all fall into the category of inappropriate
professional conduct.
recidivism came after numerous declarations that he had, in essence, changed his
way. Statements of this nature were made to
courts, the Board Inquiry, Dr Klug,
the first Tribunal and Mr Mahony. The Respondent also stated that he was doing
all he could to
take control of his business. The evidence set out above records
that the Respondent continues to demonstrate a lack of professional
The Respondent also did not put before the Tribunal evidence that he had heeded
the advice of Dr Klug and was maintaining
an appropriate medication regimen. The
Respondent has to a significant extent abrogated his professional
responsibilities to others.
His simplistic statement to Dr Klug that he would be
working as the sole pharmacist once he had his staffing at an appropriate level
is an example of his simplistic view of the deficits in his practice.
Tribunal accepts the submission of the HCCC that the events of 8 September 2011
and the sequelae thereto demonstrate that the
Respondent lacks insight into his
whole situation. The evidence before the Tribunal as to the Respondent’s
conduct on that
day and subsequently has the inevitable result of reaffirming
that the Respondent does not have the knowledge, skill, judgment or
required to practise pharmacy in an appropriate manner. Putting aside the
uncertainty as to when this matter was drawn to Mr
Mahony’s attention by
either the Respondent or Mr Cohen, it appears to the Tribunal that it was only
when the Respondent’s
lawyers disclosed information to Mr Mahony in
January 2012 that Mr Mahony became fully appraised of the matter and then, in
the Tribunal’s
view, took totally appropriate action to investigate this
matter and to admonish the Respondent for his conduct in strong terms.
concern to the Tribunal that despite the detailed analysis provided by Mr Mahony
and his strong admonishment, the Respondent
stated that he did not fully accept
Mr Mahony’s comments. Mr Mahony’s comments should be viewed in the
context of the
comments of the anaesthetist and Ms Dolan on this matter.
is nothing in the evidence before the Tribunal that indicates steps of this
nature were taken by Mr Cohen after he became aware
of the situation when Ms
Dolan visited the pharmacy on 13 September 2011. This may well have been an
opportunity lost for the Respondent
to deal with this matter at an earlier time.
In any case, the actions of Mr Mahony certainly remedied any deficit there may
been in this regard.
Respondent submitted that the circumstances surrounding the 8 September 2011
incident render it a less serious error of judgment.
The Tribunal is unable to
view the incident in this light. The circumstances relied upon by the Respondent
are not ones that would
be accepted by a registered pharmacist of good standing.
It certainly is of particular concern when it is conduct of a practitioner
currently being scrutinised by a disciplinary body. Further, the evidence before
the Tribunal indicates that Mr Mahony became concerned
the medication from merely observing the date of the previous dispensing. If the
Respondent was so influenced
by the factors he sought to rely upon to lessen the
seriousness of the conduct then the Respondent is not a suitable person to
pharmacy as he is not able to see past what he stated was his
compassion for a person’s situation to consider his professional
obligations in an appropriate manner. If the assumptions were accepted then the
Respondent lacks the ability to detach himself from
emotional considerations to
the extent necessary to apply professional consideration to decision making in
his professional setting.
may also well be, as submitted by the Respondent, that the Respondent was
subject to a ‘long standing and dedicated campaign
of deliberate
manipulation and deception’ by the patient. It is well understood in the
profession of pharmacy, as detailed
on a number of occasions in the evidence of
Mr Mahony, that there is an element amongst pharmacists’
customers/patients that
are in some manner trying to manipulate and deceive for
their own purposes. It is imperative that pharmacists be alert to these
and be able to utilise strategies to address them. Painting the
Respondent as the victim of the patient does not assist in demonstrating
the Respondent has the qualities that would allow the Tribunal to repose trust
in the Respondent to practise safely.
Tribunal accepts that the weekly supervision reports provided by Mr Cohen do
indicate that the Respondent now has the ability
to identify some of the matters
he did not identify in the past. Even having no regard for the omission of a
report regarding the
8 September 2011 issue and sequelae these reports do not
demonstrate the type of progress that would be required to overcome the
the Tribunal has identified. Many of the issues are ones that the Respondent has
indicated in the past as having been addressed
but have later been found
wanting. Some of the issues are matters that are recorded as having been raised
with the Respondent by
the supervisors put in place after the Board Inquiry and
are now being reported as being addressed during Mr Cohen’s supervision
(e.g. a method of identification of out of date stock). The evidence does not
support sustained improvements in the manner the Respondent
practises, rather,
it indicates he falls back into the ‘bad habits’ identified by Mr
not determinative of the Orders, the Tribunal notes that the Respondent’s
actions on 8 September 2011 and its sequelae
is consistent with the HCCC
submission that the Respondent demonstrates a reactive approach to practise
rather than a proactive approach.
If a health professional is merely reactive
they will encounter difficulties in their practice. This has been illustrated in
many problems encountered by the Respondent in his practice. This incident
merely confirms that Tribunal’s view that the Respondent’s
demonstrates he has significant deficits in his understanding of professional
practice, that he has a tendency to abrogate
his responsibility inappropriately.
Once again the Respondent’s evidence regarding this incident is either not
credible or
demonstrates substantial gaps in the knowledge, judgment and care of
the Respondent.
Tribunal is unable to accept the submissions made on behalf of the Respondent
that he has shown insight, real effort, and a real
capacity for change based on
the evidence before it. The Tribunal cannot accept the submission that the
Respondent is able to learn
from his errors. The implemented changes are seen by
the Tribunal as directly attributable to the intervention of others in the
Respondent’s
pharmacy. As noted above the Tribunal accepts that the
Respondent has benefited from the relationship with Mr Mahony, Mr Dash and
Piggott. The Tribunal however is making protective orders with respect to the
Respondent. It is his inability to demonstrate insight;
it is his extremely
limited capacity for change and to learn from his errors even with the extensive
intervention of others over
more than a decade that is determinative. It is the
extent of the assistance provided to the Respondent compared to the improvements
in his practice that has substantially resulted in the Tribunal finding that he
is likely to be permanently unfit to practice pharmacy.
only appropriate order for the Tribunal to make in either of these circumstances
is one of de-registration. This would be so even
if the Tribunal had not reached
the view that the circumstances of the convictions currently render the
Respondent unfit to practice
in the public interest and only having regard to
the misconduct finding.
Tribunal notes at this point that if a person with the medical and criminal
history of the Respondent made an initial application
for registration in
Australia that it would not be automatically processed. Rather, the Board would
be undertaking considerable scrutiny
of both issues.
is appropriate that the Respondent is issued with a reprimand for conduct that
has brought the reputation of the profession into
the Respondent applies for review of the orders made herein it is appropriate
for such review to be considered by a Tribunal. Further,
the Tribunal is of the
view that the reprimand issued should never be removed from his Registration
(should he regain it at any time
in the future). The Tribunal is of the view
that this is not an appropriate matter for a Board to exercise its powers under
226(3) of the National Law.
Tribunal accepts the HCCC submissions regarding costs and will order that the
Respondent pay the HCCC costs of the Inquiry held
by the current Tribunal and
any other costs not covered by the Order of Adams J. The Tribunal notes the
Short Minutes of Order found
at volume 1, exhibit 1 set out His Honours orders.
The Tribunal makes the following Orders (the Respondent is referred to hereafter
as the Practitioner):
Practitioner’s registration is cancelled fourteen days from the date of
the Reasons for Decision in HCCC v NG
[2012] NSWPHT 1.
Practitioner is not entitled to apply for review of the Order cancelling his
registration for a period of 18 months from the time
of such cancellation.
Practitioner is hereby formally and severely reprimanded in terms of Annexure C
to HCCC v NG
[2012] NSWPHT 1.

Obligation on pharmacists to comply
with legislative requirements
has long been accepted as being of fundamental importance that medical
practitioners comply with the rules regarding the prescribing
and supply of
Spicer v NSW Medical Board
(NSWCA, 19/2/1981)).
Hope JA speaking for the court said (at 3):
Tribunal expressed its opinion as to the seriousness of what was involved in
this conduct by saying that “strict adherence
to the statutory
requirements relating to the use of drugs of addiction is required of medical
practitioners” and that “the
breadth and depth of the illicit drug
problem in this community has reached alarming proportions and
any medical
practitioner who prescribes drugs of addiction other than in accordance with the
law is seriously misconducting himself
in a professional respect
my opinion that is an entirely correct approach to the matter. (emphasis
JA went on to state (at 5):
In my opinion
it is clear beyond
argument that the proper handling and prescribing of drugs by medical
practitioners are of the greatest importance
to the community. If a medical
practitioner handles or carries out that very great responsibility in a way
which is reckless and
which shows disregard to the law, it cannot be said that
he is fitted at such a time to be a medical practitioner
. (emphasis
Unsurprisingly,
a similar position applies in respect of pharmacists. In this respect see
[1998] NSWPB 8
The ownership of a pharmacy is a
privileged position. The legislation has accepted that this form of exclusive
right is granted so
as to provide maximum protection to the public. The
responsibility of ownership extends to ensure that at all relevant times,
procedures, relevant protocols and validation are in place to minimise
professional misconduct in the pharmacy.
Mr Dash succinctly stated, the past breaches by Mr Ng were a matter of concern
as he expected pharmacists to comply with their
legal obligations (XX’n of
Mr Dash, 27/7/11).

Mr Ng’s appeal to the Supreme Court, insofar as it challenged the
protective orders made by the Tribunal, was successful.
The Supreme Court
quashed the Tribunal’s protective orders and ordered that Mr Ng’s
registration be reinstated (Supreme
Court Orders, C1, Ex W, orders 2 and 4).
This occurred with effect from 5 November 2010 (R1, Ex 2). The effect of the
Court's order
is that this Tribunal must consider afresh what protective orders
(if any) should be imposed.
the Tribunal’s de-registration order Mr Ng was prevented from working as a
pharmacist in the period 5 May 2010 –
5 November 2010. Since that time,
and pending the Tribunal’s determination of the question of what
protective orders are appropriate,
Mr Ng’s practice has been subject to
limitations imposed by his Undertaking (R1, Ex 1).
is apparent from material contained in the respondent’s hearing bundles
that Mr Ng, arising from the obligations imposed
by his Undertaking, has
endeavoured to put in place steps to bring the Ryde Pharmacy and his pharmacy
practice to a reasonably accepted
standard. This will no doubt be taken into
account by the Tribunal in determining what (if any) protective orders ought to
Theodorou v Dean
[2003] NSWDC 1
at 6 of 16).
being said, the above consideration falls well short of being a complete answer
to the question of whether the Tribunal ought
to make protective orders.
recent steps taken by Mr Ng, whilst commendable, were not done for altruistic
reasons. They were essential to his attempts to
demonstrate that he is fit to
remain registered as a pharmacist. To this extent they are
ex post facto
matters. They follow, and are reactive to, the professional misconduct
engaged in by Mr Ng.
his rejection of the proposition (XX’n of Mr Ng, 10/05/2011, T 98.7 - .12)

Matters relevant to the exercise

finding of professional misconduct made by the Tribunal in
As to which see the Schedule of Findings, Particulars of Complaint

history of Mr Ng’s (repeated) non compliance with legislative requirements
(see the Chronology and the references to the
relevant material). Compliance
with such matters is inherent to the performance of the privileged role of a
pharmacist. It is of
note that Mr Ng was first registered as a pharmacist in
NSW in 1987. In 1990 he purchased the Ryde Pharmacy (C4, Ex 53, p 996).
1995 a conviction was recorded against Mr Ng for contraventions of s 9(1)(b) of
Poisons Act 1996
. Thereafter, and with concerning frequency, Mr Ng
has found himself before the courts and professional disciplinary boards;
areas of non compliance identified in the PSU’s inspection of the Ryde
Pharmacy on 15 April 2011, such as:
anomalies in the recording in the Dangerous Drug Register of the supply of
medications by Mr Ng to Patient A and Patient C.
The Dangerous Drug Register
records the supply of drugs of addiction on days when those drugs could not have
been supplied as they
were not in the Ryde Pharmacy’s Dangerous Drug safe
on the recorded day (cl 112 of the
Poisons and Therapeutic Goods Regulation
). Mr Ng’s position was that the entries made in the Dangerous
Drug Register were reflective of the date of dispensing (even
if the supply did
not occur that day because, for example, there was insufficient stock in the
Dangerous Drug safe) (XX’n of
Mr Ng, 10/05/2011, T 82.4 – 84.11;
85.32 - .36; 89.13 - .25). In contrast to the position adopted by Mr Ng, the
Tribunal heard
evidence from Mr Ho of the approach he adopted. Namely, if there
was insufficient stock to dispense a prescription then, if the
customer agreed,
he would dispense what could be dispensed from the Dangerous Drug safe, and
enter that amount as being supplied
in the Dangerous Drug Register. Thereafter,
when further stock was on hand, he would dispense the balance of the
prescription and
record that further supply in the Dangerous Drug Register
(XX’n of Mr Ho, 11/05/2011, T 150.18 – 151.3).
(2) In the case
of Patient C, Mr Ng attempted to go back and correct the Dangerous Drug Register
(R2, Ex 10, annexure NG2) and recorded
that Patient C was supplied with 100
tablets on 1 April 2011 when to his knowledge, Patient C was actually supplied
the medication
on a date (not recorded) after 6 April 2011, when the new
supplies were received from Sigma. In the case of Patient A, Mr Ng asserted

professional misconduct engaged in by Mr Ng, combined with the evidence of past

(ii) That Mr
Ng had been convicted of offences (Questioning of Dr Klug by the Chairperson,

(iii) That Mr

requirements (Questioning of Dr Klug by the

(iv) That Mr
Ng had had conditions placed on his registration (Questioning of Dr Klug by the
