being said, Dr Klug expressed the view that such additional information
“may change my opinion and it’s possible
it may not”
(Re-examination of Dr Klug, 11/05/2011, T 141.11 - .21, esp. 141.17). In short,
Dr Klug would have needed more
information.
Dr Klug’s evidence, the Tribunal will ultimately determine what, if any,
protective orders ought to be imposed based
on its assessment of Mr Ng and the
evidence otherwise placed before the Tribunal. The evidence of Dr Klug, whilst
not to be entirely
discounted, will be of limited assistance to the Tribunal in
its consideration of the matter.
Mr Ng's dilatory history; the repeated, formal reminders by the Courts, the
Board of Inquiry and the previous Tribunal of his
important professional
responsibilities, the assistance he has been given by his supervisors and
mentors to improve his practice,
the further training and accreditation
processes he has engaged in and significantly, the repeated occasions and the
long period
over which his departures from professional standards have occurred
and the most recent findings of professional misconduct, the
Tribunal could not
be satisfied that he is now fit to practice.
the above respect, Mr Mahony’s conclusion to his 27 June 2011 report (R3,
Ex 4 at p 7) that “The determination of the
success or otherwise of the
[mentoring] process must wait until this project has been completed” is
noted. Further there is
the evidence of Mr Piggott (XX’n, 28/7/11). Mr
Piggott’s evidence was to the effect that he considered that, amongst
other things, Mr Ng required oversight by a pharmacist for 1 to 2 days per week
(Mr Piggott also spoke of the need for Mr Ng to attend
conferences and
continuing education and to interact with other pharmacists). Mr Piggott
described Mr Ng as a “work in progress”,
and a person who needed to
improve his professional and commercial skills; he [Mr Ng] was not a person who
had a “free flowing”
ability to comply with the laws regulating the
profession of pharmacy (as disclosed by his previous misdemeanors).
the question of fitness to practice on the basis of the need for a pharmacist to
be overseen 1 to 2 days per week, Mr Piggott
acknowledged that the need for such
a requirement to be imposed spoke against such a person being fit to practice.
This is all the
more the case when it is appreciated that Mr Ng has been
practicing as a community pharmacist at Ryde Pharmacy for approximately
years. The need to impose a 1 to 2 day per week oversight regime on such a
person was accepted by Mr Piggott as revealing the
existence of knowledge, skill
or judgment possessed, or care exercised, below the standard reasonably expected
of a pharmacist of
Mr Ng’s level of experience.
in addition to the above, the Tribunal would not be satisfied that further
admonitions to comply with the legal and ethical
standards of his profession nor
conditions or restrictions on his practice can provide the necessary safeguards
to protect the public.
Appropriate Orders
Deregistration
with an order that Mr Ng may not apply for a review of the deregistration order
for a period of 1 year.
Ng to pay the Commission's costs of this inquiry and those of the previous
inquiry, not already covered by Justice Adams' order.

In his judgment Adams, J said:-
It is convenient to first deal
with the last-mentioned ground. The closing submissions of counsel for the HCCC
pointed to the requirement
that a pharmacist, faced with a prescription for
quantities in excess of the recognised therapeutic standards, should contact the
prescribing doctor to ascertain the justification for the proposed supply,
referred to Mr Ng’s evidence that, at least in respect
Dr Jackson’s prescription, he did speak to him about it, but
submitted that such an inquiry was insufficient in the circumstances
to meet his
independent professional obligation not to dispense a prescription in excess of
recognised therapeutic standards where
there was no good reason for doing so, a
matter as to which the pharmacist is bound to come to a positive conclusion that
necessary or reasonable, though no doubt he or she should take into
account the doctor’s opinion on the matter. At this point,
the Chairman of
the Tribunal made the following observation—
Chairman: Whilst you’re on that point ... isn’t it the
established position in relation to making notes and Mr Ng says
he rang the
doctor, made a note about it and then must have destroyed that note by following
a practice of destroying certain material
— isn’t it the case, if
there is no evidence of it, you treat it as not having occurred?
Mr Ginters: That would be my submission, yes.
Chairman: This is an established position, because of the essential
requirement of health practitioners, if they don’t note
things and the
note is not extant that it really didn’t happen.
Mr Ginters: One would have thought that if it was viewed as important
to make an annotation on a prescription or a dispensing, if
one considered that
important enough to do, the notion that then one would subsequently destroy that
prescription and destroy the
record of behaviour that was other than the norm,
in the sense of an aberration from what a script might have said, it’s a
nonsense, quite frankly.
If one wanted to come before this Tribunal and said, “Look, I had a
practice of making notations that I had contacted the doctor
things”, you would want to see that note produced before the Tribunal to
satisfy the Tribunal as to the fact that
it occurred.
Counsel then continued with his submissions to the effect that
Mr Ng’s conduct fell markedly short of that which was required
as a community pharmacist.
Counsel for Mr Ng submitted in due
course that his client did take some steps to satisfy himself as to the
propriety of the excessive
prescriptions. The following exchange then
Chairman: ... Isn’t the problem for you here that there is no
evidence of the steps taken by your client other than his statement,
are well established ways of recording these issues and there is no evidence of
the recording of these contacts, when
it is the practice to record such matters?
This has been something that has been exercising our minds, that’s why I
to give you the opportunity to make submissions about it.
Mr Griscti: I understand. It has not been put to Mr Ng that he
did not have the conversation. Mr Ng was asked by a Tribunal member
made any notation and he responded that he did, but that he no longer had it ...
[his] evidence that he telephoned the doctor
is unchallenged and there is no
basis upon which, in my submission, the Tribunal ought not to accept that
Chairman: If that is what you want your submission to be, it’s up to
us to make up our minds. It remains that it is well established
profession, as I understand it and certainly in other professions, that you make
notes of issues of this and a similar kind,
and without proof of those, the
usual approach is: well, it didn’t happen.
You say you got the evidence of your client saying he did do this, but he
thinks the material was destroyed that would have proved
it. It is worrying in
itself and it also goes against the normal practice too, to record activities
undertaken in relation to prescriptions.
It raises an issue about sloppiness in
relation to dealing with prescriptions too, doesn’t it?
Mr Griscti: It does potentially raise an issue, but as I say, it is

on his evidence that he telephoned the doctor ... the making of the telephone
call has not been challenged in
any way, and indeed he goes further to say that
in the same circumstances today he would take further steps. I can’t

Chairman: Thank you.
The Tribunal stated explicitly, as I have mentioned above, that it did not
accept Mr Ng’s evidence that he rang and queried
doctors about the
dosages and I have also set out the Tribunal’s summary of its reasoning in
respect of this conclusion.
It seems to me to be evident from the
way in which the Tribunal dealt with the seriousness of Mr Ng’s
misconduct and the genuineness
of his expressions of remorse that it was not
only influenced by the history of repeated failures to meet the proper standards
of pharmacists as well as his legal obligations and the situation
described by Mr Mahony on his inspection, but also by its conclusion
Mr Ng’s evidence on an important matter could not be believed. It is
self-evident that a conclusion that a pharmacist’s
evidence on a key
matter of professional conduct was not truthful was not only significant of
itself but must also reflect seriously
on other controversial claims as to
remorse, an issue of vital importance to considering whether continuing
unfitness and the need
for continuing protection of the public. In my view,
Mr Griscti’s submission that it was not appropriate for the Tribunal
act on the basis that Mr Ng’s evidence about this matter should be
disbelieved when there was no challenge in cross examination
to its truthfulness
was correct, certainly unless there were some unusual circumstances that enabled
such a conclusion fairly to
be drawn in the absence of such a challenge. But
there were no such circumstances and the Tribunal did not suggest their
If I may respectfully say so, the
suggestion by the Chairman arguendo that there were documents which ought to
have been in existence
which were not produced meant that it followed as a
matter of necessary inference that they did not exist — as distinct from
simply being a circumstance from which an inference of their non-existence might
be drawn — is neither a rule of fact nor law,
especially where, as here,
the witness whose evidence is impugned gave an explanation as to why the
documents could no longer be
produced, an explanation which — considering
the regrettable disorganisation of Mr Ng’s records — was not
unlikely to be true. Although it may be that the rule in Browne v
Dunn is by no means absolute, it encapsulates an important principle
fairness. Had Mr Ng’s counsel been aware that it would be submitted
on behalf of the complainant that Mr Ng’s evidence
could not be
accepted, having been alerted by cross examination to that effect, it may be
that he would have been in a position to
prove by other means that the impugned
inquiry had in fact been made. In the result, the issue was not raised until
final submissions
and, even then, the matter was raised in the form of a query
and it was not suggested to him by the Chairman that his submission
inferences which could or could not properly be drawn from the failure to
produce the written notes was not accepted...
It follows that the Tribunal’s
rejection of the truth of Mr Ng’s evidence of contacting the doctors
in respect of the
over-prescriptions was affected by a denial of procedural
fairness, amounting to an error of law.
Accordingly,
the finding that the supply of Hypnodorm was seriously unethical is not a
finding that stands. It is for this Tribunal
to determine what significance it
gives to the established complaint.
Even if this (or any other finding of unethical conduct) stands, the finding is
an ultimate conclusion and the imposition of an appropriate
order by this
Tribunal requires it to form its own judgment of the conduct, as part of its
evaluation of current fitness.

Wentworth v New
South Wales Bar Association
176 CLR 239
at 250-251). Professional disciplinary proceedings are intended
for the protection of the community, to maintain proper and ethical
professional
standards and to protect the good standing and reputation of the profession of
an order for suspension or removal is made, its purpose is “...
public point of view, for the protection of those who require protection, and
from the professional point of view, in order
that abuse of privilege may not
lead to loss of privilege”
Clyne v NSW Bar Association
104 CLR 186
at 201-202.
relevant time for determining the fitness of the respondent to practise is the
time of the Tribunal’s determination: see
A Solicitor v The Law Society
of New South Wales
[2004] HCA 1
Law Society of NSW v Foreman
, Kirby P said at 409 [10]:
... Improvement – The final consideration concerns an
aspect of the public interest to which, as I believe, insufficient attention
been paid in these cases. I expressed my views in this regard in Kotowicz v Law
Society of New South Wales (Court of Appeal,
7 August 1987, unreported). I was
there in dissent as to the result of the application. But, I hold to my opinion,
as applicable
to this case. It is an opinion which has enjoyed some favour in
other Australian jurisdictions:
‘Because the jurisdiction is for the protection of the public,
regard also may be had, to the public’s interests in the
restoration to
the Roll of such persons as have demonstrated, including by their work,
activities and life, a fitness to be restored.
For cultural and historical
reasons, redemption and forgiveness are important attributes of the shared
morality of our society. In
part, this is because of the teachings of religious
leaders who have profoundly influenced our community’s perception of
and fairness, reflected from the earliest time in the courts: see, eg.
St Matthews Gospel 18, 11ff; The Acts, 3, 19. In part, it
derives from the
self-interest which any community has to encourage the rehabilitation of those
who lapse and to hold out to them
the hope that, by diligent and honourable
efforts over a period, their past may be forgiven and they may be restored to
opinion of their family, friends, colleagues and society. The
public’s interest also includes the economic interest which is
involved in
utilising, to the full, the skills of talented people who have undergone years
of rigorous training but who, having misconducted
themselves, have had to be
removed for a time from positions of responsibility and trust.’
[51] of its decision the Tribunal, in the earlier determination, set out a
number of relevant matters to be considered with regard
to determining the
appropriate protective orders. These were:
onus of proving these matters is on the HCCC;
order cancelling the registration of a pharmacist should only be made when the
probability is that the pharmacist is permanently
fact that the pharmacist has a conviction for a serious offence is not
necessarily sufficient reason for an order deregistering
fact of conviction (and imprisonment) is, however, far from irrelevant and may
be regarded as carrying a degree of disgrace itself;
Tribunal needs to consider the conduct involved in the conviction and see
whether it is of such personally disgraceful character
that the pharmacist
should not remain a member of an honourable profession;
fact that the pharmacist pleaded guilty to the charge will usually be counted in
their favour. However, it is appreciated that
not all pleas of guilty
necessarily show remorse;
not occurring in the course of professional practice may demonstrate unfitness
if it amounts to incompatibility with the personal
qualities essential for the
conduct of practice. There may not even have been any criminal conviction with
respect to that conduct.
This is particularly so where the conduct over a long
period shows systematic non-compliance with legal and civic obligations (not
relevant in this case);
attitude of the professional association (or a peer reviewer) is of considerable
significance; and
question is present fitness, not fitness as at the time of the crime.

for more than six months, stock appeared to be overflowing on every

needed to have, so that it was a case of he was definitely below the recommended

The Tribunal considered that given the whole of the evidence of

As I have already implied, it was
significant that Mr Mahony’s inspection of Mr Ng’s
pharmacy disclosed that, so far as
he could ascertain, Mr Ng was not in
breach of any of the legal, or, for that matter, ethical obligations imposed on
him by virtue
of his being a pharmacist. The substance of his criticisms were,
leaving aside commercial considerations, that Mr Ng continued to
practical situation where some such breaches might more easily occur. A
conclusion, therefore, that Mr Ng was indifferent
to the need to act
lawfully and ethically as a pharmacist could not fairly or properly be drawn
upon the basis of Mr Mahony’s
report. The relevant legislation and
regulations imposed specific duties which, of course, a pharmacist must follow.
But, if he or
she does so, his licence to work as a pharmacist cannot be taken
away because the way in which he complies with those obligations
is thought to
be inefficient, commercially unwise or otherwise unedifying. Still less does a
pharmacist undertake not to “overwork”
— whatever that might
mean. Just because most pharmacists wear a white coat and their shops present an
attractive commercial
face to the public, and that this is what any reasonable
and sensible pharmacist would do, does not mean that wearing jeans and a
tee-shirt and working in a mess is in any way improper, of itself. Nor does it
seem to be fair or proper to reason that, because
a degree of inefficiency and
disorganization contributed to Mr Ng’s substantive misconduct, his
continuance of the messy and,
in the broader sense, “unprofessional”
mode of conducting his business meant that his evidence of remorse should be
After all, so far as his legal obligations were concerned,
Mr Mahony found that, indeed, he had changed his practices. Nor was there
any basis for inferring that, at all events, Mr Ng’s general conduct
of the pharmacy business had not improved, perhaps markedly,
for all that this
was insufficient in Mr Mahony’s opinion. It should be obvious that
dispensing drugs on prescriptions that
did not comply with the legal
requirements has nothing to do with untidiness, let alone messy
The Tribunal, in my respectful opinion,
elided the professional requirements of pharmacy with
“professionalism” in the
broader sense and failed to give adequate
consideration to Mr Mahony’s explanations of the opinions expressed
in his report.
Indeed, this evidence was not mentioned by the Tribunal. In this
respect it is worth noting, I think, that Mr Mahony’s evidence
his inspection was not at all the subject of controversy, nor was it (or could
be) suggested that his explanations were not
entirely conscientious, truthful
and unaffected by bias towards Mr Ng. It was, of course, not necessary for
the Tribunal to discuss,
let alone mention, all the evidence but where an
important conclusion is reached upon evidence whose true import cannot be
appreciated
without weighing up the explanation not referred to and those
explanations are, in substance, contrary to the conclusion drawn, it
difficult to avoid the inference that the essential character of the evidence
has been mistakenly understood.

A mitigating factor – Mr Ng’s medical
Ng was examined by Dr Klug, psychiatrist, on 30 March 2011. He provided a report
dated 27 April
oral evidence to the Tribunal. Dr Klug noted Mr Ng suffered from
panhypopituitarism since childhood, requiring him to take
hormones in order to
function properly. These include oral medication and injections. Dr Klug noted
in his report:
He said he takes his oral medication regularly but sometimes did
not have testosterone injections as frequently as he should which
caused him to
become tired and lethargic. He said that
this was the case at around the time
it was brought to the notice of the health department that he failed to complete
the DD register
appropriately
[emphasis added]
Klug expressed the opinion:
It is significant, in my view, that his problems occurred in the
context of his long standing panhypopituitarism, for which he has
been receiving
replacement hormonal treatments. He said, however, that when he was working
long hours, he was probably excessively
lethargic and fatigued because he did
not have sufficiently frequent injections of testosterone which are required on
a two to three
weekly basis. Clearly, the rate at which he was receiving the
testosterone injections can be validated through the file notes by
his treating
GP. If this was so, I regard it as a contributing factor and, without in any way
wanting to pre-empt the decision of
the Tribunal, probably a mitigating factor
in the events about which the complaint was made.
Mr Ng is now attending to his health more
assiduously
[emphasis added]
Klug gave evidence that a failure to take hormone replacement treatment would
give rise to a risk of psychiatric illness, particularly
depression. In all the
circumstances he considered it preferable that Mr Ng be under the care of an
endocrinologist. Dr Klug thought
the effect of the illness could well be lack of
motivation.
noted earlier, Mr Ng’s explanations of circumstances surrounding the
matters complained of included a reference to the fact
of this condition. Mr Ng
has not been challenged on any of that material.
Ng gave evidence that over the timeframe of the contraventions he had rarely
taken the injections as prescribed. If he was stressed
he would put it off. He
avoided the injections because of an anxiety about them.

is no basis for inferring that any of the conduct complained of in the present

the Court of Appeal has said, dishonest conduct involves matters of degree. It
is a matter on which the views of the practitioner’s
peers are entitled to
HCCC v Karalasingham
[2007] NSWCA 267
. Examples of
dishonesty will differ in their perceived seriousness and will not necessarily
require deregistration: ibid at [72]

The previous period of supervision in 2001 does not prove
that further supervision and mentoring would be ineffective
of the orders made by the Pharmacy Board on 14 February 2001 was as follows
Peter Wing Keung Ng is not to practise except under the
supervision of a pharmacist or pharmacists, approved and appointed by the
for a period of three months from the effective date of these orders. The
period of supervised practice must be at least
30 hours per week, for a maximum
of nine hours per day, over a maximum of five days per week to a total of at
least 65 days over
the three month period.
The supervising pharmacists will be pharmacist in charge at all times they
are present in the pharmacy and their responsibilities
will include every aspect
of the operations of Ryde Pharmacy. The supervising pharmacists will provide
monthly reports to the Board
which will include any recommendations or
suggestions for changes in procedures and policies in Ryde Pharmacy.
fact that Mr Ng underwent a period of supervision pursuant to this order in 2001
does not indicate that a further period of supervision,
mentoring, education and
audit would now be ineffective.
is so for at least the following reasons:
2001 supervision period was of a short duration (3 months only).
illustrated by the reports prepared by the
supervisors,
the supervision in 2001 did not have the benefit of clear criteria or
performance indicators.
supervision in 2001 did not include the setting and assessment of medium-term
and long-term goals.
fewer than 5
supervisors
were appointed in 2001, resulting in a form of supervision that was
inconsistent, unfocussed and unsustained.
the supervision was concluded, there was no appropriate “follow-up”
or monitoring by the supervisors at appropriate
intervals (eg after 6 months or
12 months), to ensure that the supervision had been effective.
supervision period in 2001 was not accompanied by any independent audit.
supervisors in 2001 were required to supervise “
every aspect of the
operations of
” the pharmacy. They did not have, as their focus, the
procedures for the receipt, storage and dispensing of drugs of addiction,
maintenance of the dangerous drugs register.
the reports of the supervisors in 2001 reveal that much of their attention was
directed to the physical aspects of the pharmacy,
its layout and state of
tidiness and to staffing levels, rather than those aspects of Mr Ng’s
practices of which complaint
is made in the present proceedings.
fundamentally, Mr Ng has now demonstrated, by his conduct, that:
has already implemented significant improvements in his practice;
has a firm resolve to continue to do so; and
has a clear insight into the inadequacies of some of his past practices, the
necessity for continuing improvements, and the reasons
Ng’s insight, and his commitment to change, are due in no small part to
the advice and instruction he has now received from
Mr Dash, Mr Piggott, Mr
Cohen and Mr Mahony, and to his time of reflection away from practice during the
recent period of suspension
following the first Tribunal decision. Mr Ng did
not have the benefit of those experiences at the time of his 2001 period of
supervision.
continuing regime of supervision, mentoring and audit is inherently more likely
to be effective in the present circumstances than
The Tribunal should impose appropriate conditions upon Mr
Ng’s continuing right to practise
together, the evidence of Mr Mahony, Mr Dash, Mr Piggott and Mr Cohen indicates
that Mr Ng is presently fit to practise and
is not a danger to the public,
albeit that he would benefit from continued supervision, mentoring and
education. The Tribunal should
fashion orders which will make Mr Ng’s
entitlement to practise conditional upon compliance with a regime which
incorporates
those elements.
Court of Appeal has said that it is “[c]learly ... fallacious” to
reason that, if a particular condition is reasonably
necessary, the practitioner
must lack a precondition to entitlement to continued practice and accordingly
the need to impose the
condition demonstrates unfitness to practice:
[2007] NSWCA 326
70 NSWLR 323

supervisor there what are they doing and why is that beneficial firstly?
A. What they would be doing is giving Mr Ng interaction with other
peers. I see examples and hear of examples from lawyers of the
difficulty of
pharmacists working in isolation, and I think it's a serious problem in our
profession, but also in all other vocations.
I see great value in Mr Ng
having the ability to mix with other professional peers and my thoughts were one
or two days a week.
I know that that can be taken to mean - that's
probably your decision to make.
I think that it would be very good for Mr Ng to - to be
aware of procedures and systems and protocols. I learn myself when I work
for - if I - if I work with a son or a daughter or something
like that, or even another pharmacist, just the way that they do things,
and - and sometimes I'll help them and sometimes they'll help me. So
I sense that Mr Ng has been in isolation and I sense that that
has - has not been good. And I believe that one or two days plus the
other things that I said; conferences, continuing education,
really for his own
good to be doing that, to be mixing.
Q. Now the second question was this; given that he's a work in progress
in your opinion, do you have a view as to how long it might
be appropriate to
impose these requirements, for example the supervision and the other
A. Well my opinion for what it's worth is that 12 months would be
of the kind, and for the duration, described by Mr Piggott fall well within the
range of conditions conventionally imposed
by this Tribunal, and the predecessor
Pharmacy Board of New South Wales, in cases of professional misconduct where, as
pharmacist has implemented improvements in his or her practice since
the time of the misconduct.
Illustrations of appropriate orders imposing conditions on
continued practice
decision of the Court of Appeal in
Lucire v Health Care Complaints Commission
[2011] NSWCA 182
provides a recent illustration of protective
orders which impose detailed conditions upon continued practice, including peer
supervision.
The appeal concerned a consultant psychiatrist in respect of whom
the Medical Tribunal had upheld a complaint of unsatisfactory
professional
conduct. The Court of Appeal set aside the orders of the Tribunal and made
orders imposing conditions on continued
practice to the effect
practitioner practise only as a member of a practice including at least two
other psychiatrists;
circumstances where one of the other psychiatrists (approved by the Medical
Council of New South Wales) undertakes to provide supervision
between the practitioner and the supervisor for at least two hours on a
fortnightly basis in the first 3 months and thereafter
on a monthly basis;
by the supervisor at such meetings of the cases of all new patients of the
practitioner and all patients with a significant
change in their condition, the
medical records kept by the practitioner, clinical outcomes, patient follow-up
and communication with
referring practitioners;
by the supervisor of a record of matters discussed at each meeting in a format
approved by the Medical Council;
by the supervisor to the Medical Council in writing and in a format approved by
the Council, within 7 days of each meeting;
requirement that the supervisor inform the Medical Council immediately of any
concern in relation to the practitioner's compliance
with the requirements of
supervision or clinical performance of the practitioner, or if the relationship
of practitioner and supervisor
Court of Appeal has recently observed that a legitimate consideration in respect
of protective orders is whether the orders made
reveal inconsistency of approach
on the part of the Tribunal, when viewed against other decisions revealing
similar kinds of misconduct:
HCCC v Karalasingham
[2007] NSWCA 267
submissions set out below some relevant decisions of this Tribunal and of the
Pharmacy Board which illustrate the range of circumstances
in which orders have
been made providing for detailed regimes of supervision, mentoring and education
in order to protect the public
in cases of professional misconduct including in
respect of the dispensing, storage and recording of drugs of addiction.
cases demonstrate that a regime involving similar elements would be an available
and appropriate means of addressing the misconduct
of Mr Ng in a manner that
protects the public and advances the objectives of the legislation.

Mr Peter Ng, you have been found to have committed professional misconduct.
You have brought yourself as a professional and the profession
of pharmacy as a
whole into disrepute. You have committed errors of both omission and
commission.
Despite greater than twenty years experience as a pharmacist your lack of
discipline and lack of accuracy in record keeping (non receipting,
recording and
misleading entries in Schedule 8 register) coupled with poor stocktaking
(Schedule 8 products) demonstrate fundamental
flaws in your practice of
pharmacy. Committing illegal actions that have brought you in front of the
courts would lead any pharmacist
to be profoundly concerned that you should be
practicing as a registered pharmacist.
The inputting of thousands of false prescription records for the purpose of
even ascertaining the increased discount that may flow
therefrom coupled with
the supply of Schedule 8 medicines on legally non-complying prescriptions that
had substantial therapeutic
risks again demonstrates flaws in your practice at a
most fundamental level.
The non-recording and inadequate recording of sales of pseudoephedrine is
particularly serious as it is of great public concern and
risk and so well known
within the profession it is hardly conceivable that a practicing pharmacist
would not have full protocols
to ensure its safe distribution to the public.
The delivery of medications, including a Schedule 8 drug, to a patient in a
public hospital on 8 September 2011 demonstrated poor
professional judgment and
caused risk to the patient and interfered with his medical care. Inadequate
enquiries beforehand and lack
of notification at the time of delivery evidence a
totally inadequate understanding of the obligations of a pharmacist in the safe
dispensing of medicines.
You have gone to some expense to address these flaws. You have employed
consultants and despite their sincere efforts there is scant
evidence that you
have made fundamental changes to your manner of practice. The inspection by the
PSU in April 2011 in addition
to the evidence at the Tribunal leaves us
disappointed that your practice is so far below the accepted standard that you
to the public.
